CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:                     India, acting by its President (GOI).
Beneficiary:                  Government of West Bengal (GOWB).
Amount:                       IDA Credit: SDR 101 million (US$99 million
equivalent).
Terms:                        Standard.
On-lendina Terms:             From the Government of India to the
Government of West Bengal as part of central
assistance for State development projects on
terms and conditions applicable at the time.
GOI would bear the foreign exchange risk.
Proiect Description:          The proposed project would comprise a five
year program for upgrading and constructing
public tubevell irrigation systems, open
dugwells, and riverlift installations. It
would support construction of about:
(i) 3,400 deep tubewells (DTWs); (ii) 5,400
shallow tubevells; and (iii) 10,000 open
dugwells (ODWs). It would finance also
completion of the distribution systems of
about 200 riverlift installations (RLIs),
improvements to management and agricultural
extension services. The project would raise
agricultural production and the standard of
living of small and marginal farmers in West
Bengal. The level of capacity utilisation of
existing minor irrigation works suggests that
there is an apparent risk of inadequate
farmer response. Hovever, the present lack
of demand for irrigation water is primarily
due to the poor quality of the irrigation
service offered. Under the proposed project,
the risk is minimized through the application
of proven designs of works and improved
technology which would ensure the provision
of a reliable, timely and equitable irriga-
tion service.

tbe offiil dubtes. Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without World Bank authorition.

INDIA
WEST BENGAL MINOR IRRIGATION PROJECT
Staff ADpraisal Report
I. BACKGROUND
General
1.01    The State of West Bengal (WB), located in the northeastern part of
India, is generally well endowed with both surface water and groundwater
resources. Irrigation is necessary for crop production during the dry season
and to stabilize production in the wet season. Under WB conditions, minor
irrigation schemes have generally lower unit area costs, shorter gestation
periods and provide more reliable sources of irrigation water than major and
medium irrigation developments. Furthermore, minor irrigation development
based on groundwater is not subject to many of the water distribution and
management problems encountered with major and medium surface water schemes.
Storage is provided naturally, conveyance from well to field is generally
over short distances, water can be tapped and delivered to farmers' fields
quickly, and irrigation is timely. Similar attributes can be described to
minor irrigation schemes based on secure surface water supplies such as river
lift schemes. Consequently, GOWB has placed great emphasis on minor irriga-
tion development, using all appropriate technologies ranging from low dis-
charge tubewells to high discharge tubewells and river-lift schemes. The
proposed West Bengal Minor Irrigation Project would support this development
strategy.
Mtriculture and Irritation in India
1.02    A detailed review is given in "Economic Situation and Prospects of
India", IBRD Report No. 4962-IN, April, 1984. India has a population of
about 750 M (mid-1984) which is growing at an annual rate of 2.0%. Since
1960, per capita income grew at an annual rate of 1.4% and has reached
US$260. Although the average per capita income has increased and access to
public services has improved, growth has been too slow to bring about more
than a gradual reduction in the incidence of poverty. Accordingly, GOI's
development plans give priority to alleviating poverty and creating employ-
ment, especially in rural areas.
1.03    Agriculture is a dominant sector of the Indian economy and con-
tributes about 33% of GNP (in 1981/82). It engages about 70% of the labor
force and provides the base for about 45Z of India's exports. During the
last decade, GOI has devoted considerable attention to agriculture in its
development plans. To support agricultural growth, GOI has: (i) accelerated
irrigation development through modernization of existing surface water
schemes and construction of new schemes; (ii) supported minor irrigation
development, particularly that based on groundwater, through direct public
intervention and through support of the long term credit system for private
use; and (iii) improved agricultural supporting services to optimize the use
of land and water resources.
1.04    Irrigation was given the highest priority from the beginning of the
planning era in 1950. Some 57.0 N ha were developed by 1980/81, about 58% of
which was from surface sources and 42% from groundwater; in recent years,



-2-
irrigated area has been expanding by about 2-3 M ha annually. Irrigated
areas are almost four times as productive as rainfed areas and account for
about 60X of all agricultural output in India. More significantly, expansion
of irrigation facilities and increased productivity on irrigated lands have
together accounted for at least three-quarters of the agricultural growth
since 1960. With little idle land left, only limited increase is expected in
the net sown area in the future. The main increase in agricultural produc-
tion will come from maximization of the irrigation potential by new develop-
ment and from full utilization of the potential in areas already developed
for irrigation, from double cropping, improvements in agricultural practices,
water management and in the use of agricultural inputs.
1.05    Groundwater development has been impressive over the last two to
three decades. The rate of creation of irrigation potential from groundwater
increased from 0.5 M ha p.a. by the end of the Fourth Plan (1968/69-1973/74)
to 1.1 ri ha p.a. in 1977/78. At present, net groundwater extraction within
India is estimated to be about one-half of mean annual recharge. The bulk
of future groundwater development must occur in the Eastern Region of India
where only about one-third of the ultimate potential has been developed. Of
the 3.5 M ha of groundwater irrigation developed during the Fifth Plan
(1973-78), about 2.2 M ha, or 62Z, were in the Eastern Region. The growth
rate of area irrigated by groundwater in the Eastern Region during this
period was 6.5% p.a. compared with 3X p.a. in the rest of India.
The State of West Bengal
1.06    Salient Features.  The State has a geographical area of about
87,850 m' and the population is about 50 M giving a population density
(570/km ) second only to Kerala within the union. The State thus contains
some 8% of India's populati 2n on only 2.7Z of its land. The agricultural
land covers about 57,000 km or 65Z of the total land surface of the State
with a rural population reported at 40 H. Most owner farmers in WB have land
holdings snich are, even by Indian standards, very small and, moreover,
extremely fragmented; there is also a high proportion of tenant farmers.
These conditions limit the opportunities for irrigation development based on
institutional credit.
1.07    The Economy.  Although the State of West Bengal is an important
industrial base of the eastern region of India, its economy is dominated by
agriculture which contributes about 40% to the State income and employs 75Z
of the labor force. The growth of the primary sector, including agriculture,
forestry and mining was less then 1.0Z per annum (p.a.) compared to an
all-India average of 2.0X. The secondary sector including jute, cotton and
tea processing industries, manufacturing, construction, electricity and
communication grew only at 1.9Z p.a., which was about half the national rate
of 3.9Z p.a. The service sector of the economy which is concentrated mainly
in and around Calcutta has contributed marginally to the vell being of the
majority of the population (80Z) living in the rural areas.
1.08    EmRloyment and Rural Poverty.  The labor force constitutes about 28%
of the WE population and cultivators and agricultural laborers constitute
about 18% and 27% of the total labor force respectively. Productive employ-
ment has not kept pace with population growth. It is estimated that 70% of
the population of WB live below the poverty level and are mainly concentrated



-3-
in the rural areas. The State land reform program aims to redistribute land
from larger farms and co register sharecroppers to establish tenants' rights.
However, implementation of the program has proved difficult.
1.09    Public Services.  The literacy rate in WB is about 41% as compared
to the national average of 36%. Availability of beds in hospitals, health
centres and clinics is about one per 758 head of the population. About 87Z
of all villages are located less than 2 km from a public water supply
provided by a dug well, tubewell or standpipe. Although the State is well
connected to the rest of India through national highways, railways and air-
ways, the intra-state communication is inadequate. The Ganges, which divides
the State, was formerly an obstruction to transport, but the Farakka Barrage
now carries both road and railway across the river.
1.10    Agricultural Production Trends (Table T-1).  About 5.7 M ha of the
State are cultivated. Paddy accounts for 92% of the cropped area and about
88% of the State's food grain production (9.0 H tons) making it the fourth
largest rice producer in India. Nevertheless, about 2.0 H tons of grain are
imported annually into WB, mainly for the 8.0 M population of Calcutta. The
State accounts for 60% of the country's jute andt25% of its tea and these two
crops are WB's most valuable agricultural exports. Production of food grains
(rice and wheat) increased at about 3.5Z p.a. to 7.4 M tons over the decade
1964-74. The greatest growth period in grain production was between 1967
and 1972 when a growth rate of 6% p.a. occurred and fertilizer consumption
increased at a rate of 20% p.a. The growth coincided with a GOWB drive to
expand minor irrigation and to introduce high yielding varieties (HYV) of
rice and wheat. The increases in grain production came mainly from irrigated
rabi crops while rainf.d production remained static. Since the early 1970's,
food grain production has fluctuated about 7.3 M tons p.a. due to variations
of monsoon rainfall and the incidence of droughts and destructive floods.
Productions of coarse grains (barley and maize) and of pulses and millets
have generally decreased over the past decade, while production of oilseeds
has more than doubled. Among vegetables, potato has recently shown the most
significant production growth rate, with an increase of about 80% above
1971-72 level.
1.11    Irrigation Develonment.  Although mean annual rainfall in the
southern districts of the plains ranges from 1,125 mm to 1,875 mm, it is
often poorly distributed in the wet season and there is considerable
inter-annual variation. Irrigation is therefore necessary for crop produc-
tion during the dry season and to stabilize production in the wet season.
The State is well endowed with both surface water and groundwater resources,
but only about 1.65 M ha (about 29Z of the cultivated areas) receive irriga-
tion. About 1.0 M ha are irrigated under major and medium surface irrigation
schemes mainly to supplement monsoon rainfall since storages are generally
lacking and rivers originating on the Central Indian Plateau have low dry
season flows. About 0.65 M ha are served by tubewells and river lift instal-
lations which provide most of the year round irrigation. The distribution of
the statewide minor irrigation development by districts is given in Table
T-2. Most public sector facilities were constructed in the decade ending
1974-75 and generally performance has been unsatisfactory. Emphasis has been
on constructing the water source (tubevell or river lift pumping station)
rather than completing the distribution systems. Even for completed schemes,
the implementation period has often been excessive.



-4-
1.12    The private sector development of minor irrigation in WB through
institutional credit has been less successful than in most other States of
India because most land holdings are small and fragmented and there is a high
proportion of tenant farmers. These conditions make access to the long-term
credit system extremely difficult, if not impossible to such farmers. In
fact, institutional credit records show a continuing decline in lending for
minor irrigation in recent years in both fiscal and physical terms in spite
of large subsidies (para 2.19).
1.13    Power Supply and Demand.  Power supply in WB is primarily provided
by the West Bengal State Electricity Board (WBSEB) with the Calcutta Electric
Supply Company (CESC) responsible for power supply to the Calcutta area.
WBSEB has a total installed capacity of about 1200 MW from various generating
stations; it is also interconnected with the grids of the neighboring States
of Orissa, Bihar, Assam and Sikkim. While the combined capacity available
between CESC and WBSEB should be adequate to meet load demands of Calcutta
and the rest of WB, restrictions through load shedding and rostering are
often enforced. However, energy consumption for agricultural purposes repre-
sents only 3% of total consumption and thus, WBSEB can give priority to the
agricultural sector without materially affecting the power supply to other
users. Endeavors are also being made to improve generation capacity and new
projects of about 1,460 MW are scheduled to be commissioned between 1984-89.
The energy is transmitted through some 4,185 km of ERV transmission (220 to
33 kV) lines and an extensive 11 kV network throughout the State. Presently
only about one-third of the villages has electric power. The Bank Group has
participated in augmenting power supply in WB through the Farakka Thermal
Power Project (Cr. 1053-IN; 1980). In addition, the Bank Group is supporting
rural electrification in WB through the Third Rural Electrification Project
(Loan No. 2165; 1982), a line of credit to the Rural Electrification Corpora-
tion (REC).
Bank Group 0perations in West Bengal
1.14    The Bank Group has supported minor irrigation development i- WB in
both the private and public sectors. The West Bengal Agricultural Develop-
ment Project (Cr. 541-IN; 1975) financed private and public groundwater
development and construction of public river lift irrigation schemes. WB
also benefitted from the four all-India ARDC line of credit projects (Cr.
540-IN; 1975; Cr. 715-IN; 1977; Cr. 947-IN; 1979; and Cr. 1209-IN; 1982)
which provided institutional credit for minor irrigation development, and
other on-farm improvements in physical infrastructure and farm equipment.
The on-going WB Agricultural Extension and Research Project (Cr. 690-IN;
1977) is designed to strengthen agricultural supporting services. In addi-
tion, the Bank (i) supports the WB Social Forestry Project (Credit 1178-IN;
1982) which is primarily designed to increase fuel wood supplies on both
private lands and degraded forest areas; and (ii) has appraised and super-
vises the Sundarban Development Project (IFAD Loan 49-IN; 1981) on behalf of
IFAD.
1.15    A predominant problem area with Bank-supported projects over the last
decade relates to organization improvements, staffing and administrative
supporting services (para 2.16). Government employees are strongly organized
in unions and staff associations, and with unemployment levels in the State,
staff resist any change that directly or indirectly may affect career
prospects or job security; organization changes can be challenged (and may be



-5-
hindered) by staff appealing to the courts for an injunction; such staff
action has caused delays to Bank-supported projects. A consequence of staff-
ing problems has been delays by GOWB in financing project works and, in
particular, admi.-istrative facilities such as buildings or vehicles which
depend on staff being in place to be fully utilized. In this respect, rather
than build and maintain buildings or buy vehicles, th- preferred solution of
GOWB is either to rent or lease.  It is concluded that mu-w of tbh problems
in completed and on-going Bank-supported projects have resulted because the
above determinants were not taken into full consideration when rhose projects
were appraised. To sumarize, a problem with development pro&rams in WB is
not only to decide what should be done, but to formulate and olan taking into
account the limitations that GOWB will have to face to make organization
changes and improvements.
1.16    With respect to minor irrigation development, only the West Bengal
Agricultural Development Project (Cr. 541-IN) undertook a component (comple-
tion of river lift installations) which will be included under the project
(para 3.27). The component was a partial success because of delays in
finalizing the planning and design criteria and a final design which was less
than satisfactory, particularly with respect to the irrigation distribution
system.  The formulation and planning of the proposed project has __ken into
account the State, sector and project issues which affected other
Bank-supported operations in WB and the lessons learnt for these projects.
II. THE PROJECT AREA AND PRESENT DEVELOPMENT
Salient Features
2.01    Location.  The minor irrigation systems to be constructed under the
project would be distributed in 14 of the 16 districts of the State, exclud-
ing only the essentially urban district of Calcutta and the mainly mountain
district of Darjeeling (Map IBRD 18610). Groundwater irrigation systems
would be distributed throughout the plains area of the State with the type of
facility selected for any location being the most appropriate taking account
of hydrogeology, physiography and land availability. The river lift instal-
lations (RLIs) to be completed under the project would be located in seven of
the plains districts. The project area is therefore representative of
climatic and agronomic conditions found within the plains area.
2.02    Climate.  Climatological data for the project area are given in
Table T-3. West Bengal has a tropical monsoon climate but proximities of the
Himalayas to the north, and the Bay of Bengal to the south, have a damping
effect on climate extremes which might otherwise be expected given the
latitude and land elevation of the plains. The winter season from November
to February is cool with typical minima of about 110C. The hot season
extends from March through May when maximum temperatures are about 370C.
About 90X of the rainfall occurs during June through October with mean annual
rainfall on the plains ranging from 1125 to 1875 mm (Map IBRD 18381) and
reaching over 2,500 mm in Himalayan foothills. There is high inter-annual
and intra-annual variation in rainfall, and within the monsoon season,
periods of drought are common. Cyclonic storms can occur which produce very



-6-
high intensity rainfall accompanied by strong winds. Uncertainty regarding
the amount and distribution of rainfall makes irrigation a prerequisite for
successful crop production even during the monsoon season.
2.03    Phvsio raphv.  WB may be divided into four physiographic regions:
(i) the Himalayan foothills region; (ii) the fringe of the Central Plateau of
India; tiii) the Sundarbans; and (iv) the plains region. The Himalayan
foothills region rises abruptly from elevations of 60-90 m to 3,700 m above
m.s.l. with deep, steep sided valleys separated by ridges of high land. The
fringe region of the Central Plateau has undulating topography with hills of
varying elevation. The plains region has gentle slopes and is crossed by a
number of major rivers. The Sundarbans region is a flat, low-lying delta
dissected by numerous natural waterways and tidal creeks.
2.04    Soils.  A wide variety of soils occur within the State
(Map IBRD 18382). These include acid brown soils of the area below the
Himalayan foothills and the acid red lateritic soils of the western part of
the State. The old alluvial soils are derived from degraded Vindyan rocks
of the Western Chotanagpur Plateau in the south-central part of the State.
The new alluvial soils have been laid down by the Ganga in the central
plains, together with deltaic coastal soils of the Sundarbans region. The
alluvial soils range from poorly to highly drained, as do the deltaic coastal
soils. The brown soils of the north and the lateritic soils are typically
medium to well drained. Thickness of soil profiles are variable and soils
may be absent on steeper slopes. Some areas of saline soils occur on the
plains area and, in particular, in the deltaic zone.  Soil fertility levels
are generally high except for marginal areas near the hills.
2.05    Land Use.  The cultivated area of 5.7 x ha represents 65% of the land
surface of the State with current fallow constituting only 1-2% of that area
in any year. The reserved forest of about 1.2 M ha. (13.7Z) is less than is
required to maintain a healthy ecological balance. Cultivable waste land,
common lands, permanent pasture and barren lands total about 0.6 H ha (7U)
mainly in the Sundarbans and Plateau regions. The remaining area (16.5X)
is not available for cultivation for various reasons such as urban and vil-
lage areas and roads, etc. The cultivated area has remained essentially
stagnant since 1974 and there is little scope for future expansion.
2.06    Land Distribution and Tenure.  Rural population density (1981)
averaged about 600/kmz of agricultural land; lowest densities occur in the
northern, western and deltaic parts of the State and the highest in the
plains and eastern areas. The agricultural work force was about 8.6 M in
1981, of which 55% were cultivators and 45% agricultural laborers. According
to the Agricultural Sample Census of 1976-77, net cultivated area per
agricultural worker ranged from about 0.4 ha in Hooghly district to about
0.8 ha in the Terai and Plateau areas (Table T-4). An estimated 5.3 M opera-
tional holdings cover 5.0 M ha and show the following farm size distribution:
Farm Size              Z Holdinsts         X Area
Less than 0.5 ha          43.6               10.6
0.5 - 1.0 E-            22.9              17.5
1.0 - 2.0 ha            20.6              29.6

-7-
Thus, farmers with less than 2 ha operate about 87Z of all holdings, but
control only 58Z of the cultivable land. In addition, fragmentation of
holdings is a serious problem. The 1976/77 census reports that typical farms
were scattered over about five fragments with an average field size of 0.2 ha
in Hooghly and 0.3 ha in W. Dinajpur. Despite progress with land tenure
legislation, many farmers are share croppers or tenants. The State land
reform program aims to redistribute land from large farms and to register all
share croppers, but implementation difficulties are great. Nonetheless, even
if all redistribution objectives in the State were achieved, the effect on
holding size would be negligible. Small and fragmented holdings and land
tenure conditions are a major reason for strong Government support of minor
irrigation development in the public sector.
Water Resources and Utilization
2.07    Surface Water Irrization.  The area irrigated by gravity systems
through government canals increased from about 0.6 N ha in 1965-66 to about
1.0 H ha in 1975-76. The gross irrigation potential from surface water
resources is estimated to be about 3.6 M ha of which some 1.3 x ha would be
from minor irrigation such as river lifts and tanks. In recent years, the
area irrigated by canal systems has averaged about 1.0 x ha, depending on
annual monsoon and run off conditions. Most government canal systems take
off from diversion weirs on rivers originating from the Central Plateau.
None of these rivers carry significant cry season flows, and consequently the
irrigation systems mainly provide water to supplement monsoon rainfall.
2.08    Minor irrigation facilities based on surface water include RLIs,
public and privately owned tanks, minor diversion canals and private low lift
pumps installed by-farmers. The irrigation potential created by minor
irrigation schemes is estimated to be about 0.9 M ha. The GOWB has con-
structed about 3000 RLIs to provide perennial water supplies. Only 1900 of
these schemes have complete piped distribution networks which serve on
average about 40 ha or about half the design command area.
2.09    Groundwater Irrigation.  Groundwater resources are contained mainly
in the alluvial aquifer system which underlies most of the plains area (Map
IBRD 18495). The system, which will support high yielding wells, is a thick
sequence of sediments in which beds of sand interfinger with lenses or beds
of silt and clay. In western WB, the alluvial system wedges out and low to
moderate amounts of groundwater are contained in Gondwana sandstones of the
Coalfield Zone. The main aquifer of the hard rock deposits is the weathered
zone and the presence of an extensive cover of lateritic mature soil indi-
cates that the zone of weathering is likely to be well developed. Other
localized aquifers on the hard rock area are the relatively thin, shoe-string
deposits of sandy river alluvium bordering stream courses.
2.10    The main recharge sources to all aquifer systems are infiltration
from rainfall, deep percolation of water held on paddy fields, and infiltra-
tion of flood water. Other recharge sources are deep percolation from sur-
face water irrigation distribution systems and infiltration from rivers,
streams, ponds or lakes. Discharge is by evaporation from shallow water
tables, transpiration of vegetation, effluent seepage to the natural drains
and abstraction by wells. Only 17% of the State groundwater resources are



-8-
presently developed with an irregular distribution within the State. Much of
the undeveloped resource is in the northern districts where the incentive
to develop groundwater for irrigation is relatively low due to the high
rainfall. Nevertheless, the present level of groundwater development
throughout the State does not constrain future groundwater development within
forseeable planning horizons. The water balance summaries by districts are
given in Table T-2.
2.11    Most of the water contained by the alluvial and hard rock aquifer
systems has less than 1,000 parts per million (ppm) dissolved solids, the
exception being the coastal areas where saline intrusion can occur. There
are considerable areas of better quality water with dissolved solids falling
as low as 250 ppm. A few pockets of water with high chloride concentrations
(in excess of 250 ppm CR) occur in some districts but their localities and
those of other poor quality groundwater are well defined.
Present Agricultural Development
2.12    Crops and Cropping Patterns.  Areas, yields and production for the
major crops in West Bengal are given in Table T-1. Taking 1980/81 as a
sample crop year, about 6.4 M ha was cultivated. Rice, grown in three dif-
ferent seasons depending on soil type and availability of irrigation water,
accounted for about 74% of the cropped area. Aus rice (9%) is grown under
rainfed conditions on the lighter and well drained soils which can be worked
during pre-monsoon rains. It is generally sown broadcast in late April/May
and harvested in early September. Aman rice (60%) is by far the most impor-
tant crop. Traditional varieties are transplanted in July/August and har-
vested in November/December; early maturing HYVs require assured irrigation
and can be harvested September/October and followed by a rabi crop. Boro
rice (5%), grown entirely under irrigation, is transplanted late January and
harvested late May. The main constraint to boro rice is availability of
reliable irrigation and high water demand in April and May. Wheat is grown
in the rabi season and represented about 4% of the cropped area in 1980/81.
However, the area to wheat has grown rapidly in recent years, particularly in
northern parts of the State with assured irrigation. The GOWB would like to
increase the areas under pulses, oilseeds and wheat at the expense of boro
rice to spread irrigation supplies in the water-short rabi season. Pulses
(7%), including pigeon peas and masur, are grown in the rabi season as
irrigated crops following rice and jute. Non-food crops account for 15% of
the cropped areas and include various types of oilseeds (4%), jute (9%) with
the remaining 2% under miscellaneous crops of which potato is the most impor-
tant. Average present cropping patterns by soil types are given on
Table T-5.
2.13    CroR Yields.  Crop yields show considerable differences by districts
and years reflecting average and actual rainfall conditions respectively
(Table T-1) and the availability of irrigation water. In general, the
average yields for most crops are higher in the plains area reflecting better
land capability, more access to irrigation and better farming practices.
Average yields for various crops in plains area districts (representing a mix
of rainfed and irrigated crop yields over the cultivated area of a district)
are given below for 1980-81:

Aricultural SuDDortima Services
2.14    Agricultural Research.  The major institutions conducting agricul-
tural research in WB are the Department of Agriculture (DOA), the Agricul-
tural University (Bidhan Chandra Krishi Viswavidyalaya), and institutes and
sub-stations of the Indian Council of Agricultural Research (ICAR). The DOA
has responsibility for conducting research to develop agricultural technology
and its research capability has been strengthened through the Bank assisted
West Bengal Agricultural Extension and Research Project (para 1.14). The
project established four commodity research stations, a new station for
wheat, and stations in the six agro-ecological zones for testing new
varieties and cultivation practices. The project strengthened the water
management research centre (WMRC) established in 1972 and also conducted
adaptive research trials at about,50 sub-divisional seed farms. Significant
achievements by DOA stations include breeding of HYV rice varieties and
research on wheat which contributed to the rapid increase in the wheat cul-
tivation in the last decade. The WMRC has established suitable irrigation
practices for all major crops on different soil types. The Agricultural
University, established in 1974, has faculties for agriculture and veterinary
science and 12 research centres including the main centre at Kalyani. The
ICAR research stations include the Jute Agricultural Research Institute
(JARI) at Barrackpore, near Calcutta which undertake breeding programs, pest
and disease coatrol programs, and research into improved agronomic practices,
and retting and fibre extraction technology. The Jute Technical Research
Institute is engaged in research on fibre quality. Other ICAR stations
include sub-centres for maize and rice, for potatoes at Darjeeling and for
plantation crops. Since the Bank has an on-going agricultural research
operation, no provision for agricultural research would be necessary under
the project. Any benefits which may result from the on-going research
program are attributed to the research project and benefits assumed in the
proposed project analysis reflect the impact on agricultural production that
would be obtained from existing research facilities in WB (para 6.05).
2.15   ftricultural Extension.  The Bank assisted West Bengal Agricultural
Development Project (Cr. 541-IN) included a component to reorganize and
strengthen the extension system in six project districts according to the T&V
system. Following this pilot operation, GOWB extended the reorganization
throughout the State with the assistance of the West Bengal Agricultural
Extension and Research Project (Cr. 690-IN). Under the T&V extension program
in WB, a comprehensive cropping program for each district is drawn up every
year with village level extension workers (VLW) responsible for assisting
farmers on regular visit days. A ratio of one VLW per 1200 farmers is



-10-
planned, but there are large numbers of vacancies both at VLW level and
amongst Agricultural Development Officers at the sub-divisional level which
may weaken the extension service to farmers.
2.16    Since inception in 1977, Cr. 690-IN has suffered serious staffing
problems which have varied in origin, scale and attempted resolution
throughout the project period. These staffing problems, are largely related
to three main factors: (i) the T&V extension concept entails the adoption of
a single-function, single-line-of-command service which involved radical
organizational changes from the traditional extension services !f; (ii) the
resistance of staff in WB to change and recourse to the courts to block
change; and (iii) the existence in WB of numerous ad hoc and inconsistent
staf5ing decisions give adequate scope for court action, even without the
added complication of a basic change in job function. A consequence of the
staffing shortages, has been a reluctance of GOWB to invest in infrastructure
and equipment essential for a viable T&V extension service. Nonetheless, the
GOWB is committed to the T&V extension philosophy and results in areas where
regular extension visits are being made, attest to the potential effective-
ness of the extension project concept. While Credit 690-IN is scheduled to
be closed on March 31, 1985, it is anticipated that WB will continue to
improve its extension services under the National Agricultural Extension II
Project. In this respect, the GOWB would undertake a comprehensive review of
the State's agricultural extension services and the project would support
incremental staffing costs for key staff required to supervise VLWs in the
districts where project works are to be located (para 3.36). Furthermore,
an agreement has been reached with GOWB that it would provide appropriate
staffing, facilities and services and other resources required to carry out
agricultural extension services throughout the districts in which the project
works are located.
2.17    Agricultural Inputs.  The GOWB has about 4,000 ha of seed farms which
supply certified seeds, but the main bulk of seeds are retained by farmers
from previous crops.  Seeds are also bought in from the National Seed Cor-
poration (NSC), the Uttar Pradesh Seed and Terai De?elopment Corporation
(TDC) and private sources (especially for vegetable seeds). In addition, the
State Seed Corporation (SSC), which started operations in 1983/84, will
supply contracting farmers with foundation seed produced on Government farms
or obtained outside the State. The SSC will undertake seed cleaning and
testing while seed certification is the responsibility of a separate
organization, the WB State Seed Certification Agency. Seed distribution is
made through the Agro-Industries Corporation (AIC), the State Cooperative
Marketing Corporation and through authorized dealers of the TDC/NSC. Nor-
mally, there is no shortage of seeds except in years of exceptional demand
due to drought or flood.
2.18    Supplies of fertilizer are generally adequate though temporary
shortages of phosphate fertilizer have occured (summer 1983). There has been
a steady increase in fertilizer use since 1977/78 with about 0.4 M tons used
in 1983/84 giving an average statewide consumption of about 36 kg of nutrient
Li The project attempted to merge 14 separate staff cadres into the Depart-
ment of Agriculture-a radical step in any public service and especially
so in a State such as West Bengal.



-11-
per ha. About 60% of pool fertilizer is distributed by the AIC and the State
Cooperative Marketing Federation. At block level, the Cooperative Marketing
Society sells direct to farmers and primary Village Level Cooperatives. In
addition, there is an extensive network of private dealers who obtain sup-
plies direct from the manufacturers. The Indian Farmers Fertiliser Corpora-
tion has a program to open outlets in more remote areas. Pesticides are
distributed by the AIC and by manufacturers mainly through private outlets.
The DOA maintains some spraying equipment for hire to farmers together with
buffer stocks of pesticides for use in emergencies. In general, pesticides
are freely available with about 3,000 tons utilized annually.
2.19    Agricultural Credit.  Institutional credit is distributed according
to the normal pattern for India. The State Cooperative Bank (SCB) provides
about 70% of short and medium term (2-5 years) credit with the balance sup-
plied by nationalized commercial banks. There are about 6,000 nrimary
cooperative societies at village level affiliated to District Cooperative
Banks (DCB). Short term credit from commercial banks is channelled mainly to
larger farmers whereas the Cooperative Banks reserve 40% of loans for
sharecroppers and tenant farmers and a further 40% to small and marginal
farmers. Short term credit is also obtained from non-institutional channels
such as traders, money lenders and family members, and through the farm
owner/tenant relationship. A major problem for short-term institutional
credit in WB, with respect to lending and recovery, is the large annual
variation in crop production resulting from climatic conditions.
2.20    The sources of long term institutional credit (more than 5 years) are
the State Cooperative Land Development Bank (LDB) and the nationalized com-
mercial banks. Traditionally, the LDB was the main source of long term
credit, but commercial banks have developed an increasing portfolio over the
past decade. Most long term lending to individual farmers has been for minor
irrigation such as tubewells, dugwells and pump sets. However, despite
government subsidies of up to 75% for water point construction and almost
100% for electrical connection, the level of lending has never achieved
programmed results. In fact, actual credit disbursement (1983/84 constant
prices) declined from Rs. 132 M in 1978/79 to about Rs. 65 M in 1981/82 and
the trend continues. Moreover, the recovery of long term credit has been
disappointing. In 1982/83, overdues to the LDB represented 61% of demand.
The evidence suggests that given the land distribution and tenure in WB (para
2.06), the demand for long term credit is limited primarily because: (i) most
farm holdings are small and fragmented or operated by tenant farmers thus
limiting the number of potential sites suitable for a privately owned irriga-
tion source which would be financially viable; and (ii) most individual
farmers with sufficient resources to develop their own wells have already
done so.
2.21    Marketing. Processing and Storage.  The State has a deficit in food
grains, mainly because of Calcutta's requirements. Only Birbhum, West Dinaj-
pur and Midnapur districts have regular surpluses to local needs and as a
result, only about 20% of paddy and 40% of wheat is sold on the open market.
In contrast about 90% of vegetables and potatoes and 98% of fibre crops are
marketed. There are about 2,600 village level primary markets practically
all privately operated; regulated markets have made only slow progress in
West Bengal. Prices at primary and secondary markets are negotiated and
farmers receive about 75% of the wholesale price of food grains and only 67Z



-12-
in the case of jute, since commodities are sold in very small lots. Cold
storage facilities (about 1.1 M tons) are available for about half the potato
crop but farmers normally sell at harvest time vhen prices are lowest.
Farmers receive about 50X of the wholesale price for vegetables with price
subject to transport costs to the main Calcutta Market. The CMF purchases
jute mainly for the Jute Corporation of India, potatoes for storage in
cooperative cold stores, and wheat for the Food Corporation of India if
prices drop to the support price. In general, there are no serious con-
straints to the present system but GOWB plans to provide more marketing
outlets to enable farmers to attract better farm gate prices.
III. THE PROJECT
Proiect Formulation
3.01    Project preparation has been carried out by the technical departments
of GOWB in close co-ordination with the Central Water Commission (CWC) of
GOI. Early 1983, the Government of India (GOI) and GOWB requested Bank
assistance regarding a project to develop deep tubewells and river lift
installations in WB; the project consisted of traditionally designed deep
tubewells and completion of river lift schemes. After visits to Uttar
Pradesh, in September 1983, GOWB revised their proposal to incorporate
improved technology similar to that tested and introduced under the
Bank-supported Second UP Public Tubewell Project (Cr. 1332-IN; 1983). In
December 1983, GOWB further decided to expand the project scope to include
construction of open dug wells and low discharge tubewells.
3.02    Proiect Concept and Obiectives.  The policy of the GOWB is to maxi-
mize minor irrigation development by exploitation of surface and groundwater
resources throughout the State. In pursuit of this policy, the project
objective would be to select the least cost and most appropriate technology
to develop the land and water resources taking into account the
socio-economic characteristics of the site to be developed. With respect to
groundwater development, the appropriate technology under the project could
be anything from an open dug well through low capacity tubewells with either
centrifugal or submersible pumps (depending on the water table depth) up to
deep public tubevells (constructed to improved design standards). Since the
command areas of the minor irrigation units would be small, locations can be
chosen so that beneficiaries include a high proportion of farmers with small
and fragmented land holdings who would not have access to the long term
credit system under the standard terms and conditions of institutional credit
(para 2.20). Thus the project would complement groundwater development in
the private sector and be selective in the target group of beneficiaries to
be reached. With respect to surface water development, the project would
provide electrical energization to pumps and buried piped distribution sys-
tems for selected, existing RLI schemes with the objective of improving their
operation and management capabilities.
3.03    A second project objective is institution building to ensure that
project works are effectively and efficiently implemented, and operation and
maintenance of completed and existing works enables predicted benefits to be



-13-
obtained. In this respect, the project would support management improvements
including a management study of the Agricultural Engineering Directorate
(AED), the main implementing agency.
3.04    Justification for Bank Involvement.  The GOWB has given priority to
minor irrigation development with emphasis on groundwater utilization (para
1.01). In the public sector, while physical implementation of the construc-
tion program has been satisfactory, its operations have not achieved expected
benefits. Poor performance has been caused by incomplete planning and design
which makes works difficult to manage, results in low efficiency and provides
an excuse for ineffective operation and maintenance. Nevertheless, in the
socio-economic context of WB and in areas with good land and groundwater
resources, minor irrigation development by the public sector is the only
effective way to reach most of the farmers who oWn or operate small, frag-
mented holdings (para 2.20). The GOWB has requested Bank involvement in the
project to assist in three main activities: (i) introduction of improved
technologies to ensure that irrigation service is timely, reliable and pre-
dictable and thus manageable; (ii) expansion of minor irrigation program
through the use of a full range of sources and technologies including shallow
tubewells and open dugwells; and (iii) institution building and management
improvements for construction and O&M of new and/or existing works.
3.05    The GOWB has decided to adopt under the project, improved tech-
nologies successfully demonstrated by the Uttar Pradesh Public Tubewell
Projects (Cr. 1004-IN; 1979 and Cr. 1332-In; 1983) for both deep tubewells
and river lift schemes. These new design and operation criteria represent a
break with past traditions and it will be difficult for WB to introduce such
changes without Bank involvement. If the project is successful, the GOWB is
likely to use the same technologies for both existing and new minor irriga-
tion works throughout the State. With respect to institution building and
management improvements, a range of improvements have been formulated during
the preparation process; such improvements as part of a comprehensive pack-
age, are unlikely to be generated within the agencies themselves without the
project. Further, GOWB has decided to increase the role of panchayats (vil-
lage level elected councils) in the program; Bank assistance has helped GOWB
to formulate the necessary safeguards to minimize risks and yet take full
advantage of this innovative management move.
Project ComRonents
3.06    The proposed project would finance a five year program
(1984/85-1989/90) incorporating the following elements of the GOWB develop-
ment program in the minor irrigation sector:
(a)  DeeR Tubewells.  Construction of deep tubevell irrigation systems
to serve about 67,000 ha including: 3about 1,200 high capacity
tubewells (HDTWs) each of about 200m /h capacity and serving abojt
40 ha; 400 medium capacity tubewells (NDTWs) each of about 100 m lh
capacity and serving about 20 ha; and about 1,800 low capacity
tubewells (LDTWs) each of about 30 m /h and serving about 6 ha.
(b)  Shallow Tubewells.  Construciion of about 5,400 shallow tubewell
systems (STWs) of about 30 m lh capacity and each serving about 6 ha.



-14-
(c)  Open Dug Wells.  Construction of about 10,000 open dug wells (ODWs)
of various designs in alluvial and hard rock areas.
(d)  River Lift Installations.  Completion of the distribution systems
and conversion to electric powered pump units on abSut 200 existing
river lift installations (RLI) each of about 400 m /h capacity and
serving about 80 ha.
(e)  Manatement ImDrovements.  Provision of equipment and buildings for
AED staff; research and development to test advanced technologies for
public tubewells and RLIs; training and study visits and tours for
project staff; preparation of Design and O&M Manuals including tech-
nical assistance; and studies related to project management, cost
recovery and an agro-economic baseline for selected project areas.
(f)  Strengthening the Asricultural Extension Services.  Incremental staff
costs and preparation of a comprehensive plan for agricultural exten-
sion services for districts under the project.
Planninz Criteria
3.07    Site Selection Procedures.  The land and groundwater resources in WB
have been surveyed in sufficient detail by the State Water Investigation
Directorate (SWID), to select broad areas where the project works would be
technically and economically viable; the preparation work has included an
evaluation of existing and proposed power supply and demand. Thus, with
respect to State level selection, a GOWB Committee has already identified
development areas within the State and has made a general allocation of the
project program to the various districts. This could be done because the
proposed work program is small compared to the potential development area and
available groundwater resources. Project impact areas for groundwater
development have been decided on general physical and technical cuaisidera-
tions but at district level there remains considerable flexibility in select-
ing sites of individual works. District level selection will be made by
District Site Selection Committees (DSSC) which will include representatives
of the concerned district level officers and the panchayat organization. The
DSSC will identify local areas which are likely to meet siting criteria for
individual works and also decide priorities under the work program within the
district. Once the local area has been identified where works can be sited,
individual site selection will be made by village panchayats following strict
selection criteria so that physical, technical, socio-economic and management
objectives can be achieved. In particular, individual site selection would
ensure that potential beneficiaries understand and are prepared to accept the
responsibilities expected from them. Once a specific site is identified,
site investigations by AED would be made to confirm technical viability.
Choice of each individual site at local level would then be reviewed and
confirmed by the appropriate committees at district and state levels. In
this way, the various agencies involved in project would be able to check
that siting criteria has been adhered to, and would be fully informed of the
proposed implementation program and schedule.



-15-
3.08    Siting Criteria.  For new DTW, STW or ODW systems, well sites will be
located such that groundvater availability would be sufficient to provide a
reliable irrigation service as assumed in project design; soils in the com-
mand area would be of suitable quality for project agricultural objectives to
be achieved; the command area would not be subject to submergence during
heavy floods; and there would be no other public irrigation service in the
areas selected. In addition, for DTW and STW systems, an individual well
site would be a maximum distance of 1.5 km from an existing 11 kV power line
and the average distance of all project well systems from existing 11 kV
lines would be not greater than 1.2 km. For locations which comply with the
above technical criteria, the type of well to be installed would be the
least-cost technology appropriate to the land and aquifer conditions of the
area. Once the general location and type of well have been decided,
individual command areas would be determined to satisfy the following
socio-economic and management criteria:
(i) the construction of private tubewells is difficult because of the
drilling depth necessary to achieve a satisfactory yield; and/or the
area is economically backward and further private well construction
will be very slow to develop;
ii)  at least 80X of the command area of any DTW or group of STWs, and
100% of the command area for ODWs would be owned or operated by
farmers with holdings less than one hectare;
(iii) the command area of project works would not contain holdings with
private irrigation sources (excluding open wells) except for high
capacity DTWs, where not greater than 1OZ of the command area is
served by private irrigation sources; and
(iv) the local panchayat has the capability and would agree to take on
the management responsibilities as defined under the project (para
5.11) for the well type selected for the site.
The above criteria can be strictly enforced since command area boundaries
and/or water point locations can be adjusted to ensure that a selected target
group of beneficiaries are reached. Nonetheless, special attention will be
given during the selection process to those areas which could be developed by
either STWs or DTWs to avoid pre-emption of a possible private development
option and to ensure that the least-cost design alternative is selected.
3.09    The locations of the RLIs to be modernized with piped distribution
systems and electric motor powered pumps have already been selected; the main
criterion has been proximity of an 11 kV power source which can be extended
to serve the pump station.
3.10    Water Requirements.  The wide geographical distribution of the
project works imply cultivation under a range of climatic and soil conditions
and varying proximity to markets; consequently, there will be a wide varia-
tion of cropping patterns throughout project lands. In almost all cases, a
wet season rice crop will be taken mainly for the farmer's home consumption.
This could be either a traditional 'aman rice grown on rainfall in most
years, or a wet season HYV which would require at least puddling and
pre-planting irrigation in some years. The HYV rice crop would be preferred



-16-
if vheat is to follow due to its early maturing characteristics. Many
farmers will grow ,'boro' rice, a dry season rice, with very high water demand
and a typical peak requirement of about 10 an/day in April on tight soils.
us' rice, planted during the pre-monsoon rains, would normally require
irrigation in widely varying quantities depending on the actual rainfall
year. Jute has similar water requirements to 'aus' rice less the puddling
requirement. Wheat and potato would be grown as rabi crop entirely under
irrigation with water demand for potato somewhat higher that wbeat.
3.11    Weighted mean water requirements have been estimated.  Based on this
analysis, all tubewell irrigstion systems and BLIs are designed with pumping
capacity equivalent to 100 mi/h on 20 ha of command area. This converts to a
water duty of about 0.92 1/sec/ha (equivalent to 8 rm/day on the command
area) with a daily operation of about 16 h/day. Crop peak water requirements
are given during land preparation and pre-irrigation for 'aus' rice and
equate to about 14 rn/day through April. 'Boro' rice produces a peak demand
in April of about 9 mm/day. The project is designed to support about 50X
crop cover of  Aus' rice or ILOZ crop cover of 'boro' rice grown on tight
soils. During peak periods, it is assumed that wells could operate up to
about 18 h/day through April and IBSEB confirm that for short periods, this
level of power service can be provided. No other crops are constrained by
the water supply.
3.12    With respect to open dug wells in hard rock areas, the governing
constraint would be well yie d. An average dug well equipped with a pump set
would produce about 12,000 m  of water over the five months period of the
dry season. This is equal to a water supply at well head of 500-600 am/ha
for a 2 ha command area; which is adequate for 100% cover of vegetables; the
well would also be used to provide supplementary irrigation to wet season
rice. The water duties on typical dug wells in riverine alluvium (serving
about 4 ha) would be similar to the tubewell systems (para 3.11).
Deep Tubewell Systems
3.13    Well CaRacity.  Relatively deep tubewells (DTWs) gould be the
appropriate technology where: (i) yields over about 100 m /h are required;
(ii) aquifer conditions such as clay layers in the upper soil profile make
DTWs necessary for all yields; and (iii) deep water levels preclude use of
SIlWs with centrifugal pumps. Well design and drilling technology would be
similar for all DTWs irrespectivS of discharge capacity. The preferred
discharge of a DTW would be 200m /h serving about 40 ha since such a well
would give the least cost per unit area developed. however, there are parts
of the State where contiguous command areas of 40 ha may not be available.
For such areas, an medium capacity well (MDTW) of 100 m /h serving 20 ha or a
cluster of low capacity wells (LDIW) each of 30 m3/h serving about 6 ha may
be suitable. Since, individual well sites will be selected as the project
proceeds, allocation of the DTW program under the project between high,
medium and low capacity wells is provisional. Bowever, enough sites will be
selected before the mid-term review of the project (para 3.38) when, if
necessary, allocation of the DTW program will be revised.
3.14    Design of liuih and Medium Capacity Tubewell Systems.  The design of
HDTW and MDIW systems follow the principles successfully tested under the
UP Public Tubewell Projects (para 3.05). A system would consist of a water



-17-
point with pump house; a distribution chamber equipped with float svitch for
automatic control of the pump; a buried pipe distribution system with outlet
valves each commanding about 2 to 3 ha; earthen field channels below the
outlets to serve farm holdings; an access road to the water point for opera-
tion and maintenance purposes; and an electrical power supply. The improved
tubewell systems are described below with detailed design principles given in
Annex 7, SDV; the estimated costs of typical DTW systems are given in
Table T-6.
3.15    The Water PiJnt.  The water point would consist of the well, pump,
prime mover, switch gear, starter and protection relays and electrical power
connection, all located in a brick-built pump house. The wells would be
fitted with submersible or electric turbine pumps. Water level operated
electrical probes fitted at the distribution chamber would permit automation
of the pump prime mover. The electrical system would be provided with a
capacitor, low voltage or temperature cut-out, and a single phase preventer
in the starter relay to protect the motor. Electronic hour meters would be
fitted on all pumping units. The wells would be drilled using either direct
rotary or reverse circulation drilling rigs. Total depths of wells would be
in the range of 60 to 250 m according to the design, discharge capacity and
local site condition. Well casing and screen would be of mild steel or other
approved material; under the R&D component (para 3.33), other materials would
be tested. Materials required and unit cost estimates are given in Annex 3,
Tables I and 2.
3.16    Distribution Chamber.  An essential feature of the system is the
elevated distribution chamber which maintains operating head in the piped
distribution system (Chart C-1). It also enables automatic operation of the
pumping unit by means of level operated electrical probes which: (i) activate
power supply according to the water level in the chamber and in response to
irrigation demand; and (ii) enable water distribution at less than the full
discharge rate of the pump without spillage. The design of the distribution
chamber ensures equal division of the water supply from the well into two or
three streams depending on the design capacity of the water point.
3.17    Buried PiDe Distribution System.  A typical layout for a HDTW dis-
tribution system is given in Chart C-2. The buried conveyance system would
be PVC feeder pipes from the elevated distribution chamber to connect with
closed loops or branches (where indicated by shape of service area) of PVC
pipe which constitute the main distribution system. The distribution chamber
would ensure equal division of the pump discharge to each pipe loop or
branch. The loop design ensures that an outlet can be supplied from two
directions which reduces flow velocities and head losses in the pipelines.
A EDTW system would have three loops while a MDTW would generally have two
branches. In EDTW systems, feeder pipes would be either of 200 mm diameter
PVC or reinforced concrete; distribution pipes would be 140 mm diameter PVC.
The P9T systems would have 110 mm diameter PVC pipe. Pipes would be 2.5
kg/cm pressure rating with a depth of cover not less than 0.8 meter. The
average lengths of feeder and distribution pipes for the two system types are
given in Annex 3, Tables 1 and 2.
3.18    Distribution Slatem Outlets.  Outlet valves would be provided along
the buried distribution pipes to command about 2 to 3 ha ses ving 5-10
farmers. The valve would be designed to deliver about 67 m Ih under the



-18-
prevailing head at any point in a EDTW distribution loop or about 50 m3/h on
a MDTW branch. Only one of about six outlets on each loop or branch would be
operated at any time. The valve would be set in a brick-built distribution
box which would have an arrangement to deliver water in several directions as
required. A single 200 mm surge riser with a top elevation about 0.5 m above
the top water level of the distribution would be provided at a point farthest
from the chamber.
3.19    Field Channels.  The most distant field in an outlet command would
generally be less than about 160 m from the outlet valve. Common field
channels would be aligned along property boundaries such that water can be
delivered from the outlet distribution boxes to individual farm plots within
the outlet command. Average length of earth channel for typical HDTW and
IMDTW command areas would be about 50 i/ha.
3.20    Power SuDPp1. The HDTW and NDTW systems would be provided with an
electrical power supply by extending an 11 kV feeder line to the well head
and providing an 11/0.4 kV transformer and a short low tension connection to
the pump unit starter relay. The maximum length of llkV spur line would be
1.5 km, with an average length of 1.2 km for all DTWs wells under the
project. Dedicated 11 kV lines serving only state owned wells would not
normally be required as power supply is generally reliable in WB. Nonethe-
less, to ensure that project wells can function as planned, agreements have
been reached with GOWB that through the WBSEB; it would: (i) provide project
tubewells and RLIs with 16 hours of power supply per day at peak demand
periods with a voltage fluctuation of not greater than + 6Z; and (ii) ener-
gize each project tubewell or RLI within two months of its completion.
3.21    Low Capacity Deep Tubewell Systems.  Individual LDTWs each discharg-
ing about 30 m3/h and serving about 6 ha would be sited in clusters of about
six wells for effective management and to reduce operator costs. The wells
would be located where water level requires a shaft driven turbine or submer-
sible pump. An LDTW system would consist of water point, an earthen channel
delivery system, and an 0.4 kV low tension power line.
3.22    The water point would consist of the well, pump, prime motor, switch
gear, starter and protection relay, and electrical power supply, located in a
brick-built pump house. The starter relay would be direct on-line and a
capacitor would be fitted for pump motor protection. Electronic hour meters
would be fitted to facilitate monitoring of pump operation. The well. would
be hand drilled with a pump chamber of 200/150 mm diameter to an appropriate
depth and a production string of 80 mm diameter to total depth. The wells
would be gravel packed and have a depth of about 60m. Earthen field channels
would be aligned along property boundaries to deliver water from a well to
farm fields; the most distant field would generally not exceed 300 m from the
well head. The average density of field channels would be about 80 m/ha of
command. An cluster of LDTWs would be provided with an 11 kV power line to
a 11/0.4 kV transformer at the load center of the cluster, from where lower
tension 0.4 kV connections would be made to each well head. The lengths of
11 kV line to the transformer location for each cluster would average about
1.2 km with about 0.22 km of low tension lire per well. The unit cost of a
typical LDTW is summarized in Table T-6 and detailed in Annex 3, Table 3.



-19-
Shallow Tubewell Systems
3.23    In areas with shallow water levels, tubewells equippel with
centrifugal pumps would be constructed discharging about 30 m /h and serving
about 6 ha. In such conditions, shallow tubewells (STWs) would be preferred
over LDTWs due to their lower cost and easier maintenance of the pump unit.
The STWs would be grouped in clusters of about six wells and energized
through 0.4 kV low tension power line from a common 11/0.4 kV transformer on
an 11 kV spur line. A STW system would consist of a water point (well,
centrifugal pump, electric motor, and on-line starter relay), pump house,
earthen channel water distribution system from well head to the farm fields,
and the power connection. Apart from the well and pump unit, the STW system
is similar to the LDTW system (para 3.21). The wells would be hand drilled
at 150 mm diameter for installation 80 mm diameter pipe casing and screeo,
and a course sand pack. The wells would be up to about 60 m depth and about
9 m of screen would be provided. The centrifugal pump mounted either at land
surface or in a sh2llow sump would be powered by a 3 HP electric motor and
provided with a small hand pump for priming. Cost estimates for typical STW
systems are summarized in Table T-6 and detailed in Annex 3, Tables 4.
Dug Wells
3.24    Two types of dug wells would be constructed under the project - a
large diameter well for hard rock areas, and a small diameter well lined with
precast concrete rings for areas where relatively thin but highly permeable
riverine sands overlie hard rock. Both well types would be suitable for
installation of a centrifugal pump.
3.25    Duz Wells in Hard Rock.  The typical open well would be about 3m
square cross section and about 12 m deep and would serve about 2 ha. Brick-
work will normally be required to secure the uppermost part of the well where
soil and unstable rock may be encountered; the remaining well section would
be unlined. The well will penetrate below the water table to provide a
reservoir for pumping; construction of this section will require continuous
dewatering. As each ODW can be pumped only for a few hours each day under
production conditions, if it as decided to mechanize wells, one diesel driven
centrifugal pump with 30-50 m /h discharge could be shared by a group of
wells. The water distribution system from well head to the farm fields will
be earthen channels. Unit cost estimates for wells are given in Annex 3,
Table 5.
3.26    Dug Wells in Riverine Alluvium.  The wells will be excavated at about
1.5 m diameter for installation of 1.2 m internal diameter precast, rein-
forced concrete rings with well depths extendi.g to about 12 m. When the
water table is reached, the rings will slide down as excavation proceeds and
dewatering would be required when working in this section. A well will be
completed when a fully dewatered section cannot be maintained for further
excavation with a 100 m3/h pump. Such wells would sustain a continuous
discharge of about 30 m3Ih when fitted with centrifugal pumps powered by
either diesel or electric motors. The command area would be about 4 ha and
the water distribution system from well head to farm fields would be in
earthen channels. Unit cost estimates for wells are given in Annex 3,
Table 6.



-20-
River Lift Installations
3.27    The GOWB has installed over 3,000 small scale RLIs during the past
two decades throughout the State. Most RLIs use diesel motor pump units and
many have incomplete water distribution systems and pump houses. Under the
project, about 200 existing RlIs would be modernized and completed by:
(i) electrification of the pump units to enable the RLI to be operated
automatically as for improved HDTWs (para 3.16); and (ii) provision of
elevated distribution chambers, a buried pipe water distribution system with
outlets to 2-3 ha commands and an earthen channel conveyance system within
outlet commands to farm fields. Many design characteristics of a modernized
RLI would be similar to those described for HDTWs and MUTWs (see para 3.13
et. seq.) differing only because the stream size (400 el/h) and command area
(80 ha) of the RLI would be larger. The systems are described below with
detailed design principles given in Annex 7, SDV. Cost estimates are sum-
marised in Table T-6 and detailed in Annex 3, Table 7.
3.28    The Water Point.  A typical water point would consist of the pump
units (pump, prime mover, starter and protection relays, electrical power
connection) all located in a brick built pump house or on a barge for float-
ing pump units. The pumps would be two 200 m13h, electric motor driven,
centrifugal pumps with Star-Delta starter relays wired for independent opera-
tion. The electrical system would include a capacitor, low voltage or tem-
perature cut-out, and a single phase preventer in the relay for motor protec-
tion and electronic hour meters. An 11 kV spur line would be extended from
the nearest 11 kV line to an 11/4.0 kV transformer located at the pump house.
The transformer would be connected to the pump starter relay by short LT
lines and would include an electricity meter. The length of 11 kV spur line
would average 2.5 km per facility.
3.29    Distribution Chambers.  The pump station discharge of 400 m3/h would
be divided into six equal streams of about 66 a3/h, each serving sub-systems
of the distribution system which can be operated independently (see Chart
C-2). This would be achieved by installing a primary distribution chamber on
the river bank near the pump station where a first division of the flow would
be made, followed by one or more secondary distribution chambers located at
strategic positions in the command area where final division of flows would
be made. The breakdown of the full flow at the primary chamber 3iay be into
flows of 66 m3/h or multiple.; of this discharge (typically 132 m /h) accord-
ing to the geometry of the command area which controls the optimum pipe
layout. The primary chamber operates as a master chamber for the system to
control flows from the pumps and automate of the pump switch gear. The
design principles of distribution chambers are similar to EDTWs (para 3.16).
3.30    The Distribution System.  The buried pipe conveyance would consist of
RCC pipe in the larger sizes (450 mm and/or 300 mm diameters) and PVC pipe in
the smaller sizes (200 mm, 160 mm and 140 mm diameters). The system would
consist of feeder pipes leading to delivery pipe sub-systemc which could be
closed loops or branches. Pipe outlets would be valves installed in
brick-built distribution boxes and commanding 2-3 ha as described for BDTWs
and NDTWs (para 3.18). Earthen channels would be aligned along property
boundaries to deliver water from the outlet to individual farm plots
(para 3.19).



-.21-
&ananement Imurovements.
3.31    The project would support management improvements within the Agricul-
tural Engineering Directorate (AED), the main implementing agency
(para 5.02). Work units would be established for quality control and train-
ing; the existing research and development and-monitoring and evaluation
(M&E) units would be strengthened. The project would also provide for a
Management Study of the AED (para 5.10); construction of residential build-
ings and a small staff training center; equipment; preparation of Design and
0&M manuals; visits and study tours for project staff; and technical assis-
tance. The project would also finance additional extension staff required to
supervise and support the VLWs in the 14 districts of IB included under the
project. The estimated costs of these items are given in Table T-7.
3.32    Buildinas.  Both residential and non-residential buildings are
provided as standard items under all construction programs with the number
and size of the buildings based on long established statewide norms. The
buildings used during construction are handed over to 0& staff when works
are commissioned. Non-residential buildings are used for management purposes
and storage facilities for O&M supplies and equipment. In recent years, the
statewide development program has expanded and in most districts, the AED has
very little accommodation and rentals are expensive and limited. As a conse-
quence, particularly in more remote parts of WB, the AED has difficulty in
attracting qualified field staff. Therefore, under the project, residential
buildings would be constructed for selected grades of staff in 0&M and other
field activities. The inspection bungalows would be standard PWD designs
and generally constructed on land presently owned by the AED or GOiB.
3.33    Research and Development.  The design concept and criteria of the
project works are acceptable to IDA. However, the nature of the project
components are such that design standards and specifications can be reviewed
on a continuous basis -and improvements introduced where necessary. To assist
GOWB in planning for future developments, the project would undertake studies
and test improved irrigation technologies and techniques which are either
untried in WB or have only been attempted on an experimental scale.  The main
activities to be tested and evaluated have been provisionally identified in
conjunction with GOWB and would include further technical improvements to
minor irrigation systems and procedures to minimize cost and/or improve O&M
standards of minor irrigation management. The initial work program would
be finalized by the R&D unit within the AED (para 5.07) which would be
strengthen under the project. The project would also support the cost of
experimental civil works (Table T-7). Agreements have been reached with GOEi
that through the AID: (i) not later than December 1, 1985, it would prepare
and submit to IDA a draft work plan for research and development activities
under the project and taking into consideration IDA's comments, carry out the
work plan; and (ii) in consultation with IDA, commencing July 1, 1987, review
annually the work plan, and if necessary, revise such work plan accordingly.
3.34    Technical Services.  To help the AED in the preparation of Design and
0&M Manuals (para 5.23), technical assistance would be provided through about
8 man-weeks of consultancy service. The monthly cost including salaries,
fees and subsistence for a foreign consultant would average about US$12,000.



-22-
3.35    Traininz. The project greatly expands the State program of con-
structing minor irrigation systems which involve planning, design and
implementation concepts which are novel to most of the AED staff. Conse-
quently, the Government would have to undertake substantial training to
correspond with the staffing required to achieve the project implementation
schedule (para 5.22). The project provides for one small service training
centre, training equipment, visiting local specialists to assist the AED
staff in giving the various training programs, and also for tours and visits
to enable project staff to widen their experience.
3.36    StrenRthenina the Extension Services.  To help the State consolidate
the progress made in improving its extension sevices (para 2.16), the project
would finance the incremental staffing costs of key extension staff (mainly
Agricultural Development Officers) in the Department of Agriculture (DOA)
required to supervise and support the activities of VLWs in the districts
under the project. In addition, the GOWB would undertake a comprehensive
review for further strengthening of its extension services in the State. In
this respect, an understanding has been reached with GOWB that it would
prepare and submit to IDA for its review and comments, a comprehensive report
summarising its plans for agricultural extension services in West Bengal over
the project period, and taking into account IDA's comments, if any, revise
such plan accordingly. In this respect, the plan for extension services
would, inter alia, cover staffing required at each level, training needs,
equipment, vehicles and civil works. It was further understood that the plan
would be prepared in a format and a timely manner which would enable it to be
considered for financing under a Bank supported National Agricultural Exten-
sion Project should GOI/GOWB make such a request.
Project Implementation (Chart C-3)
3.37    The project would be implemented over a five year period from about
March 1985 to March 1990. The main construction season is from mid November
through mid June although certain activities can continue through the monsoon
season at some sites. The construction work would be distributed extensively
throughout the State. All components have established construction techni-
ques which relate to local conditions. Much of the work is suited for small
contracts and sufficient labor contractors are available throughout the State
to ensure satisfactory progress and good quality work.
3.38    An agreement has been reached with GOWB that it would locate and
carry out the project works in accordance with siting, planning and design
criteria, and construction standards agreed by IDA. In addition, agreements
have been reached with GOWB that it would: (i) prepare and provide to IDA
for review draft terms of reference for a mid-term review of the project and,
taking into account IDA's comments, carry out a mid-term review of the
project; (ii) submit a report summarizing the results of the review to InA by
March 31, 1988; and (iii) taking into account the Association-s comments,
carry out necessary adjustments to the project.

The foreign exchange cost would be about US$ 13.4 million, or 9% of the total
project cost. Cost estimates are based on price levels prevailing in Septem-
ber 1984. Physical contingencies on civil works have been provided for each
type of construction activity (for example, to allow for variations in the
depth of the wells and layout of the distribution systems which depend on
local aquifer conditions and topography respectively). The physical contin-
gency average about 6% of the base cost. Price contingencies, which account
for about 24% of the base cost, reflect expected inflation rates over the
project period (same rates for local and foreign expenditures), and have been
taken as 8.0% in 1984/85, 7.0% in 1985/88 and 6% thereafter.

been reached with GOI that it would make the proceeds of the credit available

to the States. Agreements have been reached with GOWB that it would promptly
provide resources and funds to: (i) execute the project in accordance with
the implementation and expenditure schedules for the project period, and
