4.04    Works to be financed under the project would cost approximately
US$ 44.3 M, excluding materials and equipment (para 4.06), power supply (para
4.05) and engineering and administration. About US$ 28.3 M (65Z) would be
let through Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) contracts, and the remaining US$
16.0 H would be let under piece-work contracts or be carried out by GOWB's
departmental forces. All LCB contracts for civil works would be let on the
basis of documents and procedures approved by IDA.
(a)  LCB Contracts (US$ 28.3 H).  These works would not be suitable for
international tendering since they would be individually small,
scattered over a large area, and carried out intermittently as deter-
mined by weather conditions and agricultural activities. They would
include construction of deep and shallow tubewell systems, modern-
ization of river lift installations, access roads and buildings.
Generally, the materials and equipment under these LCB contracts
would be supplied by the State Government.
(b)  Advance Contracting (US$ 0.5 H).  Works contracts will have been let
between September 1984 when the project was appraised and April 1985
when the project is expected to start. These contracts would be let



-25-
after LCB on the basis of CWC's standardized procedures and
guidelines. An estimated US$ 0.5 M worth of works would be outstand-
ing under such contracts after April 1985 and would be eligible for
financing from the proposed credit.
(c)  Other Procurement Procedures.  (USS 16.0 M).  These works would
include field channels and dug vells; as for LCB contracts, these
works would be individually small and scattered and their implementa-
tion would be governed by weather and agricultural conditions.
However, unlike works under LCB contracts, the works would be so
small that there would be a general lack of interest from contractors
located outside the immediate areas covered by the project.  These
works would therefore be impractical for ICB or country-wide tender-
ing and most of them would be carried out under small piece-work or
rate contracts. When required by safety or quality considerations,
or when the quantities of work are difficult to estimate in advance,
certain small works (estimated in aggregate at US$ 2.0 N) may be
implemented by GOWB's departmental forces.
4.05    Power SuRDlv.  Power transmission and transformation works to be
financed under the project would cost approximately US$ 33.1 M, excluding
engineering and administration. Of this, about US$ 16.3 M would consist of
installation works and US$ 16.8 M would be materials and equipment. Of the
installation works, about US$ 13.3 M would be let after LCB contracts with
the remainder (uSS 3.0 M), which would include storage and transportation
costs, undertaken by the WBSEB's own forces. Since these works would be
small and scattered throughout the area, they would not be suitable for
international tendering. As with IDA credits for rural electrification,
WBSEB would be responsible for the works including procurement of its own
project materials using Rural Electrification Corporation (REC) standard
specifications, General Conditions of Contract and procurement procedures as
agreed for the on-going Bank supported Third Rural Electrification Project
(Loan 2165-IN). Generally, WBSEB would not procure materials and equipment
for project works under separate contracts but include the project items as
part of their regular procurement for the statewide rural electrification
programs. Thus the WBSEB would invite tenders, using LCB procedures satis-
factory to the Bank, for the procurement of conductors (US$ 6.0 M), distribu-
tion transformers (US$ 2.5 M), ST insulators (US$ 1.5 M), concrete poles
(US$4.4 M) and general materials (US$2.4 M). In view of the expected large
number of small contracts and the present smooth functioning of the procure-
ment process, only contracts exceeding US$0.5 M equivalent would require IDA
approval prior to contract awards. The WBSEB procurement procedures for
materials procured when not using IDA Credit proceeds are satisfactory.
4.06    Materials and Equipment to be financed under the project would cost
approximately US$ 49.9 M, excluding engineering and administration. Stand-
ardized procedures and bidding documents approved by IDA would be use- 4or
procurement of materials and equipment. The submersible pump units and
ancillaries for RLIs and DTIs (US$ 12.1 N) and PVC pipes and fittings (US$
14.6 M) would be procured through ICB procedures except for initial quan-
tities of materials to be used in the first construction year (US$ 1.8 M and
US$ 1.5 M of expenditures on pump units and PVC pipe and fittings respec-
tively) which would be procured under LCB procedures. A preference limited
to 15Z, or the prevailing customs duty if lower, would be extended to local
manufacturers in the evaluation of bids. The equipment and materials woald
be grouped to promote efficiency and attract competition. However, bids will



-26-
also be accepted on individual schedules to attract small manufacturers and
also reflect the scattered nature of the project works. Centrifugal pump
sets and ancillary equipment (US$ 3.4 X); steel pipe, fittings and casings
(US$ 14.8 M); general steel worl- (USA 2.4 M); and reinforced concrete pipe
(USS 2.2 M) would be procured through LCB procedures. The equipment procured
by LCB would benefit from existing servicing and supply facilities for spare
parts which need to be available over the 14 districts covered by the
project. For both equipment and material procured by LCB, there is adequate
domestic competition and prices are highly competitive. Equipment for
management improvements (USS 04 M) would be procured as part of the general
GOWB procurement for Goverument agencies.
4.07    Extension Services.  The incremental staff salaries and allowances
of the agricultural extension staff (USA 0.9M) would not involve procurement.
4.08    Contract Review.  All bidding packages for works estimated to cost
USS0.5 M equivalent or more, and for goods costing US$100,000 equivalent or
more, would be subject to standard IDA pre-action review. These will account
for at least 80% of the procurement value of works and 90% of the goods. The
remaining contracts would be subject to IDA post-action review.
Disbursements
4.09    Disbursements would be made for: (i) 100% of the foreign expenditures
for directly imported equipment; (ii) 100% for locally manufactured equipment
procured ex-factory or 70% of cost where ex-factory price is not available;
(iii) 100% of expenditures for mAnagement improvements (excluding buildings)
and for the agricultural extension component; (iv) 75% of expenditures on
buildings and for completed works (excluding materials procured under items
(v) and (vi) below) for deep tubevells, river lift installations, shallow
tubevells and open dugwells; (v) 75Z of expenditures on steel and reinforced
concrete pipes, casings and fittings and general steel materials; and
Cvi) 10OZ of expenditures or 100% of ex-factory price for PVC pipes and
fittings and submersible pumps and ancillaries. Disbursements for works
constructed by departmental forces and for payments of less than Rs 300,000
for works and Rs 150,000 for goods itemized by sub-project, and for extension
staff costs may be made against certificates of expenditure.
4.10    Disbursement for completed shallow tubewells and open dug wells would
be made against certificates of expenditure submitted by GOWB, itemized by
major components including the cost of energization. The disbursement
request for completed deep tubevells or RLIs would also include a certified
statement by the Superintending Engineer in charge that: (i) all works have
been completed in accordance with the agreed siting, planning and design
criteria; and (ii) the tubevell or RLI has been electrified. Documentation
for these works would be retained by GOWB and made available for inspection
by IDA during review missions. Full documentation would be required for all
other disbursements.
4.11    The estimated schedule of expenditure is given in Table T-8.  The
semi-annual disbursement schedule and the proposed allocation of the proceeds
of the credit are presented in Table T-9. Only expenditures occuring upto
March 31, 1990 will be eligible for reimbursement and it is expected that
disbursements would be completed by March 31, 1991. The schedule of O&M



-27-
expenditures is given in Table 8, Annex 3. The expected rate of disburse-
ments for the first three years of project implementation follows the typical
disbursement profile in the Indian irrigation sector, after which, the rate
of disbursement remains constant since implementation of project components
would probably continue under the AED long term development program.
Accounts and Audits
4.12    The project would be subject to normal Government control and audit-
ing procedures. A resident Audit Officer, representing the State Accountant
General (AG) would audit the project accounts. Although continuous and
annual audits are carried out in a timely manner, the finalization is a
lengthy process during which the Statutory Audit Report is presented to the
State Legislature. It would be essential therefore to have interim state-
ments of account certified as to accuracy and authenticity by an independent
competent auditor. Such statements, certified by the AG or his designate,
supported by an itemized account and a summary of the expenditures, would be
submitted to IDA as soon as possible after the end of each fiscal year.
Agreements have beer. reached with GOWB that it would cause the AED, the DOA
and the WBSEB to: (i) maintain separate accounts on project expenditures kept
in accordance with sound accounting principles and procedures; (ii) have
these accounts and financial statements certified as to their accuracy and
authenticity annually by independent auditors acceptable to IDA and make the
resulting interim statements of account available to IDA as soon as possible,
but not later than nine months after the end of each fiscal year; (iii) have
the project accounts audited annually in accordance with sound auditing
principles and submit the audit report to IDA as soon as available; and
(iv) make available complete accounts and financial statements to IDA for
inspection during project review. In aadition, an agreement has been reached
with GOWB that it would cause the AED to maintain separate accounts for O&M
expenditures in such a manner that the O&M costs of works constructed under
the project can be identified.
'V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
General
5.01    Once the irrigation service can be measured and made predictable
through improved technologies, management can be made more effective. Per-
formance of management staff can be evaluated and beneficiaries can assess
whether they are getting the service promised. In particular, management
improvements can be structured to focus on specific measures to set standards
of responsibility and accountability and introduce incentives in management
procedures. Thus, under the project, GOWB plans to: (i) strengthen the
agencies responsible for execution and management of the project works;
(ii) establish procedures to use village panchayats as 'management agents'
for the shallow tubewell and open dugwell components; and (iii) establish
procedures to monitor progress of project implementation and realization of
project benefits.



-28-
Project Organization
5.02    Overall responsibility for project implementation would rest with the
Secretary for Minor Irrigation (MIS). The Agricultural Engineering Direc-
torate (AED) under his jurisdiction, would be the main agency responsible for
project implementation; the AED constructs, operates and maintains river lift
installations and public groundwater irrigation schemes throughout the State.
The WB State Electricity Board (WBSEB) is responsible for construction,
operation and maintenance of power transmission and transformation under the
project. In addition to the construction agencies, the Department of
Agriculture (DOA) would be responsible for the agricultural extension com-
ponent and for the agricultural development activities after minor irrigation
works are commissioned. The WB State Water Investigation Directorate (SWID)
would be responsible for assessing surface and groundwater resources and for
clearance of any groundwater development. The Department of Panchayats (DP),
through village panchayats would be involved in the project as 'management
agents' for the AED (para 5.11).
5.03    Proiect Co-ordination and Accountina.  The Chief Engineer of the AED
would be appointed to act as Project Co-ordinator (PC), reporting directly to
the Secretary of Minor Irrigation (Chart C-4). He will be responsible for
the day-to-day coordination at the working level between the participating
agencies. The PC would be assisted by a Project Co-ordinating Unit (PCU) and
a Project Accounting Unit (PAU) which would be set up exclusively for the
project. The PCU will draw up annual implementation and development
programs; prepare project reports and summaries of monitoring and evaluation
studies; and formulate and maintain an information link to project
beneficiaries and other agencies involved in the project. The PAU would work
closely with the PCU and would be responsible for project accounts and
accounting; requests for project financial provisions; and collation of
expenditures for reimbursement from IDA. Agreements have been reach.d with
GOrB that by June 30, 1985, it would: (i) set up and thereafter maintain a
Project Co-ordinating Unit and a Project Accounting Unit within the AED; and
(ii) appoint a suitably qualified and experienced Officer to head the Project
Co-ordinating Unit and act directly under the Project Co-ordinator.
The Agricultural Enzineering Directorate (AED)
5.04    Existing Organization.  The main implementing agency would be the
AED, which was set up with agricultural engineering rather than minor irriga-
tion as its main activity. It was structured with two separate professional
cadres - Agricultural-Irrigation (A-I) and Agricultural-Mechanical (A-X) --
with demarcation of posts within the organization to reflect recruitment
qualifications and thus protect career prospects of the staff in each cadre;
this has resulted in an inflexible organization structure which cannot effi-
ciently respond to variations in the AED work load. The (A-I) cadre has been
made responsible for tubewell operations and the (A-M) cadre for river lift
installations (RLIs); staff from one cadre do not undertake work allocated to
the other cadre. In addition, there is no formal separation of construction
and O&M activities within the AED; consequently it is difficult to pinpoint
responsibility for O&M works and administrative costs of various AED
activities cannot be easily identified. At field level, most existing works



-29-
cannot provide a reliable and predictable irrigation service; since perfor-
mance cannot be checked, inefficient management practices have developed
partic- ulary overmanning amongst DTI and ELI operators who are strongly
unionized.
5.05    The AED is headed by a Chief Engineer (CE) assisted by an Additional
CE. At State level, operating from the Calcutta headquarters, there are
three units, each headed by a Superintending Engineer (SE), responsible for
Investigation and Planning, Resources Procurement, and Monitoring and Evalua-
tion; in addition, there is a Design Division headed by an Executive Engineer
(EE). In the field, there are four (A-T) Circles and four (A-M) Circles with
territorial responsibilities for both construction and O&M activities. The
SEs have responsiblity for a circle; a SE oversees four to six EEs who each
head a division. The EEs are assisted by two to seven Assistant Engineers
(AE), each responsible for a sub-division. Typical divisions are structured
and staffed based on financial workload norms of Rs 10 M per year for con-
struction or about Rs 4.0 M per year for O&M activities.
5.06    Proposed Or2anization.  The GOWB has recognized the problems within
the AED and concurrent with introduction of new technologies, plans to
improve the management process. This will require short and long-term
actions. As a first step, construction and O&M activities would be divided
such the responsibility of individual staff and work units can be fully
defined in terms of physical workload. Second, organization and staffing
requirements would be estimated using physical rather than financial norms to
define the workload which could be efficiently undertaken by a fully staffed
division or sub-division (Annex 9, SDV). The organization structure over the
project period has thus been formulated in terms of the sub-divisional
requirements for construction and O&M for each type of irrigation system
implemented or managed by the AED. A typical division is assumed to have
four sub-divisions which could be either construction or O&M. The provi-
sional organization build-up is given on Table T-10 and shown on Chart C-4.
5.07    At headquarters, the existing Investigation, Planning and Design
(IPD) Wing would be strengthened under a Chief Engineer (Agriculture) to
manage statewide activities. Within the IPD Wing, there would be five units
involved in project activities. An Investigation and Planning (MP) Unit
would coordinate all aspects of planning, formulate project principles and
criteria and set construction specifications. The existing R&D unit would be
strengthened and be responsible for the R&D component (para 3.33). The
existing Design Unit would be strengthened and would prepare detailed or type
designs for all project works. The I&P and Design Units would also be
responsible for the Design and O&M Manuals to be prepared under the project
(para 5.23). The existing Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) Unit would be
strengthened (para 5.25). A Project Training (PT) Unit would be established
for in-staff training (para 5.22) and for organizing study tours provided
under the project. Also at headquarters, the existing Resources Procurement
(RP) Unit under a SE would be strengthened and be responsible for all
procurement under the project. The RP unit would also be expanded to include
a Quality Control Unit responsible for all aspects of quality control during
the construction and commissioning phases of the proiect program.



-30-
5.08    At field level, each of the (A-I) or (A-M) Circles would have three
or four divisions divided into construction or 0&M sub-divisions depending on
workload within the territorial comand of the circle. A typical construc-
tion sub-division would be responsible for drilling wells, constructing pump
houses and distribution chambers, installing pumping equipment, laying out
pipelines to valve outlets, and construction of field channels. It would
also be responsible for commissioning new project works and would design and
supervise the setting up of the rotational system of water distribution in
each well or RLI command area (para 5.17).  The O&K sub-divisions would be
responsible for all aspects of O&M of project DTWs and RLIs (para 5.20). A
group of about 25 deep tubewells (or 25 clusters of tubewells with each
cluster commanding 36 to 40 ha) would be supervised by a Sub-Assistant
Engineer (SAE); in some cases, an SAE may be responsible for a central divi-
sional store and vorkshop. The project vorks are scattered and for O&M staff
to function effectively, transportation would be provided (either owned by
the AED or leased) with at least one jeep per division with O&M responsiblity
and motorcycles for AEs and ASEs primarily responsible for O&M.
5.09    The AED staffing requirements through the project period are shown
in Table T-11. Project staff would be available either through transfer
within the AED or through recruitment, depending on overall staffing require-
ments of the AED through the project period. Agreements have been reached
with GOWB that it would: (i) sanction and fill staff positions and provide
the necessary funding in accordance with an organization structure and staff-
ing schedule acceptable to IDA, such that the AED can carry out the project
program; (ii) sanction and deploy sufficient O&H staff so that the project
works can be operated and maintained in a manner satisfactory to IDA; and
(iii) train such staff so that the AUD can carry out the project program
and operate and maintain the project works.
5.10    In the long-term, more fundamental structural changes may enable the
AED efficiency to be improved beyond that achieved under the sort term
actions (para 5.06). To facilitate such improvements, the GOWB will retain
qualified Management Consultants to undertake a comprehensive Management
Study of the AED in accordance with terms of reference agreed with IDA
(Annex 4). In this respect, agreements have been reached with GOWB that it
would: (i) undertake a Management Study of AED in accordance with terms of
reference agreed with IDA; (ii) submit a report summarizing the results and
recommendations of the study to IDA for review by September 30, 1986; and
(iii) taking into account IDA's comments, to carry out the recommendations of
the study according to an agreed timetable. One of the main tasks under the
study will be to evaluate the (A-1) and (A-M) cadre issues within the AED
(para 5.04), and formulate practical actions to resolve the problems. Fur-
thermore, the study has been scheduled so that before the recommendations are
finalized, locations of new works under the project will be fixed in suffi-
cient detail to finalize the organization structure and staffing requirements
at the local level. This information would be available before December
1985, and thus recommedations of the Management Study would be based on
detailed siting of works and a specific work program through the project
period.



-31-
Proiect Manatement
5.11    Role of Panchavats.  An important element of the GOWB rural develop-
ment strategy is to increase the role of panchayats (village level elected
councils) in the program; the panchayat system is described in Annex 9, SDV.
The panchayats in West Bengal are strong, well organized and hold regular
elections. The GOWB wants to take advantage of this grass root organization
to help overcome lack of motivation and flexibility within line departments
and eliminate inefficiencies which often occur when government departments
undertake minor work programs spread over large areas. The GOWB argues that
local panchayats have a strong incentive to satisfy as many beneficiaries as
possible since panchayat committees are elected bodies and each farmer has
a vote. In addition, panchayats are primarily interested in performance of
works within their territory and are thus able and prepared to enforce
accountability. Using panchayats as 'management agents' would be an effec-
tive way to break the power of government employed operators -- one of the
most important causes of inefficient operation at present.
5.12    The essential safeguard would be to retain the AED in a supervisory
role with the right to take over direct management if a panchayat proved
unable or unwilling to do an effective job. In addition, any accounts
managed by panchayats and used for project activities would be audited on a
half yearly basis following established Panchayat Department procedures with
audit reports submitted to the AED for review and approval. Thus, the risks
associated with using panchayats as ,'management agents' for project works are
small since the panchayats' role would be carefully defined and limited to
their proven capability.
5.13    Deep Tubewells and River Lift Installations.  The improved technology
for EDTWs, MDTWs and ELIs would consist of automated w.tll or pump source and
a closed pipe distribution system serving valve outlets or spouts which are
opened and closed by farmers (para 3.18). The technology enables groups of
farmers served by any spout to assess whether they are receiving their cor-
rect allocation of water in terms of quality and quantity. Stream size is
fixed by the technology and so the only variable which has to be monitored is
the time and timing of their turn. Since the systems would be farmer
operated, record keeping and reporting breakdowns woul_ become the main role
of the AED operator. Furthermore, since records kept by the operator can be
cross-checked, for the first time in WB, his performance can be independently
monitored. The improved technology will enable GOWB to employ less qualified
operators with only one operator employed at each site instead of an operator
and assistant at present; thus the O&M costs of operating staff will be
considerably reduced compared to those of existing works. With respect to
new and existing DTWs and RLIs, GOWB may use local panchayats as paymaster
for any operator employed by AED within the panchayat jurisdiction. At
present, operators are paid through the local Block Development Officer (BDO)
who cannot check if the operator is working effectively. The proposed arran-
gements would mean every AED operator would be paid through the local elected
body representing the beneficiaries being served.
5.14    Shallow Tubewells.  The STW technology is relatively simple and the
limited O&M activities can be undertaken at local level. The STWs would be
owned by the AED which would use the local panchayat to manage the wells.



-32-
O&M funds would be allocated to the panchayat to pay for the energy bills,
maintenance costs and for one operator (to be known as an Irrigation Assis-
tant) per cluster of wells recruited by the panchayat. The panchayat would
also keep records of revenue from water charges, which would be collected
by the revenue authorities through appropriate collection channels to be
decided by GOWB; initially water charges would be set at the same level as
for existing works. The GOWB proposes a three year transition period during
which the panchayat performance would be supervised and monitored by the AED.
Once the local panchayat has proved that it is responsible and capable, the
STWs would be fully handed over. Panchayat involvement in the management
structure will introduce responsibility, accountability and incentives at the
local level. Beneficiaries will have direct access to staff responsible for
the various activities and, in particular, the operator can be checked by
both his direct supervisor (the Panchayat Committee) and the farmers he
serves. The management structure also requires that records be kept and
evaluated at the local level including expenditure and revenue information;
this will encourage accountability of paid staff (and indirectly the AED)
since inefficiencies would reflect on village development finances. The GOWB
is also considering other incentives to reward efficiency; for example, if
panchayats operate and maintain their scheme effectively, keep within budget
allocations and maintain complete records on a regular basis, they would be
allocated more minor irrigation development. Should a panchayat fail as
management agent, the AED would take direct responsiblity for O&M and action
would be taken through the Department of Panchayats to sort out the problems
or strengthen the local panchayat. In this way, a secondary project benefit
would be to test and strengthen village panchayats for other local rural
development.
5.15    Open DuRwells. The ODWs would be sited with AED technical assistance
and construction would be organized by the panchayat (samity level) super-
vised by AED. Initially, one cluster of ODWs would be allocated to each
qualifying panchayat. Within the comand area of each well, every farmer
would be given an equal share to the well irrespective of land holding size.
Either farmers would be given the opportunity (and be paid) to undertake any
unskilled labor necessary to build the well or, alternatively, local labor
contractors would be used. A siting condition of the well would be farmers-
agreement to these conditionalities. Farmers would be encouraged to use
savings from the money earnt to purchase (or make the down payment for) a
,mobile' diesel pump set to be commonly owned by the farmers served; alterna-
tively, the panchayat might purchase a pump. In this way for most clusters
of ODWs, the farmers will have a pump set when wells are commissioned. The
'farmer construction scheme is attractive because it provides employment
opportunities in areas where jobs are seldom available before labor require-
ments build up on the irrigated areas.
5.16    The ODW scheme would be easy to monitor by the AED since each
panchayat and group of beneficiaries would be given a detailed plan of
action. The arrangement has obvious built-in incentives for the
beneficiaries. The incentive for the panchayats would be an offer of funding
for further ODW clusters if they successfully complete and bring into opera-
tion the first cluster of ODWs; it is expected that local pressure from other
farmers who want wells will encourage panchayats to perform effectively. It
is also expected that the ODW program would encourage institutional credit
activity in areas of WB which have been difficult to reach. Long-term credit



-33-
would be needed for purchase of pump sets for those well clusters where labor
earnings are insufficient to buy the pump set outright; short term credit
would be needed for other agricultural inputs.
Operation and Maintenance
5.17    Water Allocation.  At present, in IB, public minor irrigation works
are operated 'on-dmand'.  Cultivators make a pre-season application (and
pre-payment) for water for specific areas of nominated crops.  The area of
crop actually irrigated is checked periodically during the season.  Water
supply generally exceed demand but shortages can occur when the whole command
is under ,boro' paddy in the rabi season. Such shortages are dealt with on
an ad-hoc basis often to the detriment of the weaker farmers or those fur-
thest from the water source. The improved distribution systems for project
works will enable allocation or entitlement to water on the basis of area of
holding. Payment of water can thus be based on quantity used, measured in
terms of hours of delivery to the cultivator. The GOWB proposes to continue
with the 'on-demand' water allocation but in periods of water shortage to
impose allocation proportional to holding size. The GOWB also proposes under
the R&D program to test and evaluate water allocation and distribution on a
per hectare basis, with water charges based on actual water use (para 7.16).
5.18    Operating PrinciDles.  Operation of DTWs and RLIs would depend on the
method of water allocation adopted. Where this is by application of
individual cultivators (as at present), schedules would be established for
each outlet based on authorized areas and type of crops to be irrigated by
the cultivators supplied by that outlet. Distribution within the group of
cultivators (generally not more than ten per outlet) would be set by estab-
lished schedules, but with considerable freedom of interchange between neigh-
bors. During periods of shortage, water supply would be allocated on the
basis of area of holding. The method of operation successfully demonstrated
under the UP Public Tubevell Project (Cr. 1332-IN) would be followed: namely
to organize cultivators served by a distribution loop into seven groups, each
taking water for a fixed day of the week (either from a single outlet, or
from two or more outlets sequentially if more convenient for the group com-
mand area). Water distribution within the group would be largely decided by
the group members. The operation of MiDTWs would follow the same principles
as HDTWs. Low capacity DTWs and STWs would operate as for a group of farmers
served by an outlet on a large tubewe-ll. Each farmer would take the whole
water supply in turn either on a 'demand' or 'allocated supply' basis depend-
ing on whether supply exceeds demand.
5.19    Some degree of interruption of delivery schedules due to transmission
line breakdowns or power outages is to be expected. However, with reliable
power supply in most parts of WB, interruptions should be infrequent enough
to be accomodated without change of basic rotational schedules to outlets.
5.20    Maintenance Criteria.  Each group of about 25 DTWs or RLIs would be
under the responsibility of a SAE who would have the continuous services of
skilled mechanics to provide the maintenance service for individual tubewells
or RLIs. Each 06& work unit (para 5.08) would have access to an adequately
equipped workshop for maintenance of pumps, prime movers and switch gear,
plus adequate transport. Workshop stores would generally hold at least 10Z



-34-
spares against pump turbines, prime movers and ancillary electric equipment.
As part of routine maintenance and at half yearly intervals, pump discharges
would be checked and turbine impellors replaced if low discharges indicate
wear; well discharge and drawdown would also be checked and tubewells
rehabilitated if the discharge/drawdown relationship indicates deterioration
of performance.
5.21    Farmers  Organization. An importan- task of the SAE in charge of a
cluster of tubewells (DTWs and STWs) or BLIs would be to assist in organizing
farmers. Farmers within each area served by a delivery point would form a
spout committee of three representatives to supervise water distribution
within their command area as per authorized schedule and elect a repre-
sentative to represent them on the Tubewell or RLI Committee to be set up
under the guidance of the GOWB. The Tubewell or ELI Committee would elect a
President and a Secretary and would be responsible for coordination and
cooperation among spout committees, for solving disputes, and for liaising
with concerned SAE (OM) responsible for the installation or with the local
panchayat committees where STlWs are managed by panchayats.
Other Proiect Management Activities
5.22    Training.  Staff training organized by the Project Training Unit
(para 5.07) would be required for: (i) planning and design of improved
tubewell and river lift systems; (ii) water allocation, land acquisition
procedures and legal parameters affecting project works; and (iii) the O&M of
completed works. Training would be necessary because the automatic well
operation and pipe distribution systems under the project are unfamiliar to
many engineering staff. A pre-requisite to the training program would be the
compilation of the Design and O&M Manuals. The training would be
,in-service', directed by senior AED officers and other experts.  The courses
would include lectures, discussions, case studies, and fiel_ trips. Junior
staff would be trained for about three weeks and senior staff for one week.
For senior staff, courses in improved management procedures would be prepared
under the AED Management Study (para 5.10). Details of the training programs
are provided in Annex 11, SDV.
5.23    Design and O&M Manuals.  The improved minor irrigation technology
systems have been demonstrated under the UP Public Tubewell Projects. Thus,
sufficient information is available to prepare: Ci) a comprehensive Design
Manual which would record planning principles and design criteria for the new
tubewell technology together with construction practices to implement the
works; and (ii) an O&M Manual covering all aspects of management of public
groundwater systems. The manuals would also describe arrangements b3 which
the AED relates with other agencies involved in minor irrigation development.
A provision is made under the project (para 3.34) to assist in the prepara-
tion of the Design and O&M Manuals. An agreement has been reached with GOWB
that it would prepare and submit to IDA for review by December 31, 1985,
drafts of comprehensive Design and O0N Manuals, suitable for field staff
responsible for planning, design and construction and for operating and
maintaining tubewells and RLIs in the State.



-35-
5.24    ProRress Review and Reporting Requirements.  Progress reports would
be prepared by the M&E unit (para 5.07) and be submitted to IDA on a
quarterly basis. Each report would contain brief descriptions of the main
events of the reporting period including construction progress; expenditures
incurred; project staffing status; tenders, evaluation and award of con-
tracts; irrigation potential created; actual areas irrigated; operational
hours of water points; and the power supply situation. Agreements have been
reached with GOWB that it would submit to IDA: (i) project progress reports
on a quarterly basis, commencing October 1, 1985 and within two months after
the end of each reporting period; and (ii) a project completion report, no
later than six months after the closing date of the project.
5.25    Monitorinz and Evaluation (M&E).  The existing M&E unit would be
strengthened at headquarters under the CE (Agriculture) and three field units
set up at regional level (para 5.07). The M&E unit would be responsible for
collecting and analyzing data on project planning, implementation and perfor-
mance particularly with respect to siting of project works and the quantity
and quality of the irrigation service. The unit would also be responsible
for agro-economic monitoring, and undertake data collection for the main
crops under the project during the growth cycle including crop production and
input/crop yield data. The operator at each tubewell or river lift system
would assist the M&E unit by keeping daily and seasonal records which reflect
aspects of agricultural performance. Details of the M&E requirements of the
r-oject are given in Annex 11, SDV.  Agreements have been reached with GOWB
that through the AED, (i) not later than September 30, 1985, it would prepare
a draft work plan for M&E activities under the project; (ii) taking into
consideration the Associations' comments, carry out the work plan; and
(iii) commencing July 1, 1986, review the work plan annually in consultation
with IDA and, if necessary, revise the work plan accordingly.
5.26    One of the main N&E tasks will be to carry out baseline studies to
enable project impact to be evaluated. The studies would be undertaken in
three stages.  First, an initial baseline study of the present situation
would be made for representative sample areas to be developed with each type
of technology under the project. Data would also be collected outside the
project area so that the 'future without' project situation can be assessed.
Second, the areas evaluated under the initial baseline study would then be
monitored on a regular and routine basis by the M&E staff. In this respect,
the M&E unit would prepare regular reports of its activities after data from
each kharif and rabi cropping season has been processed. An important
monitoring task will be to verify that the project target groups (para 3.02)
are indeed reached. Finally, in the year prior to project completion, data
collection under the initial baseline study would be repeated so that project
impact can be evaluated. The conclusions with supporting data of the final
study would be submitted as part of the Project Completion Report (para
5.24).
5.27    To assist the GOWB with M&E activities under the project, the AED
would retain either qualified local Consultants or an appropriate government
agency to: (i) undertake the initial baseline survey and based on the survey
conclusions assist AED formulate a M&E program to be undertaken by M&E staff
on a regular basis; (ii) advise on preparation of data collection sheets;
frequency of data collection; sample selection and sizing; evaluation



-36-
methods; management use of proceeded data; and feedback procedures so that
results can be past back through the various levels of AED staff; and
(iii) assist the AED with agro-economic studies required for the Project
Completion Report. In this respect, agreements have been reached with GOWB
that it would: Ci) carry out a baseline agro-economic survey the project
with terms of reference established in consultation with IDA; (ii) submit to
IDA for its review and comments, by July 31, 1987, a report summarizing the
results and conclusions reached under the study; and (iii) taking into
account the IDA's comments, carry out any further monitoring and evaluation
recommended under the study such that project impact can be fully evaluated.
West Bengal State Electricity Board (WBSEB)
5.28    The WBSEB is responsible for the construction and O&M of rural elec-
trification works in the State including the connections to public tubewells.
An Additional Chief Engineer (ACE) plans and monitors the progress of both
general RE works and also special schemes financed by the Rural Electrifica-
tion Corporation (BEC). At field level, where there are sufficient REC
works, WBSEB positions an Assistant Engineer (AE) with responsibility only
for construction of these works. At present, if a new public tubewell falls
within a special REC scheme area, energization of that well would be under-
taken as part of the REC scheme; in areas outside a REC scheme, the AE (O&M)
in whose jurisdiction the new tubewell is located is responsible for the
connection as part of his routine O&M activities. Supervision of all con-
struction works is carried out by the OEM Executive and Superintending
Engineers under the control of the zonal Chief Engineers.
VI. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION. MARKET PROSPECT AND PRICES
Cropping Patterns
6.01    Experience with minor irrigation development in WE has shown that
cropping patterns would generally follow present practices with choice of
crops depending on soil types, availability of irrigation water, and for cash
crops, access to ready markets. The main crops are described in para 2.12
and present and expected future cropping patterns are shown in Table T-5.
Present cropping patterns and yields are based on data collected by either
the AED or Revenue Department; future cropping patterns are based on perfor-
mance of farmers with predictable irrigation supplies. Choice of crops with
reliable irrigation depends primarily on soil types, the three dominant types
being: Ci) heavy soils which are low lying but not subject to flooding;
(ii) medium textured soils in the main potato growing zones; and (iii) light
soils of the higher lands which are generally sandy loams to loams.
6.02    In the kharif season, for all soil types, aman paddy would be the
main crop; limited areas would be put to vegetables, sesame or mung where
there is access to a good market. Kharif paddy requires supplementary
irrigation in years of low rainfall or when there are breaks in the monsoon.
Availability of a reliable irrigation service would encourage farmers to grow
early HYV varieties of paddy (100-120 days) transplanted in July. In the
rabi season, most crops would be fully irrigated; paddy would be grown on the
heavier soils but it is expected that wheat would become a more important



-37-
crop. The harvesting dates of early kharif paddy would enable an earlier
planting of wheat and other rabi crops with a resultant increase in wheat
yields and decrease in water requirements. On medium soils, in the rabi
season, potato is a major cash crop, particularly in areas with cold storage
facilities or access to ready markets. In the pre-kharif or hot season, on
medium and light soils, jute would be the main crop with aus paddy on the
heavier soils. The double cropping of paddy on heavy soils eliminates the
chance of a hot season crop.
6.03    The 'future without' project situation would be the same as the
,'present' situation and takes into account siting criteria for project works
under which most beneficiaries would be small or marginal farmers.  It is
assumed that these farmers would continue to follow rainfed cropping patterns
even if they have access to protective irrigation by renting water from
owners of private irrigation sources. The irrigated area from private sour-
ces is difficult to evaluate in terms of the project analysis because, at
present, any area that receives even less than one complete watering is
recorded as ,'irrigated.'
6.04    Cropping patterns for the 'future with' project situation take into
account five major factors: (i) the preference of farmers to grow paddy;
(ii) the area irrigated from private sources; (iii) the capacity constraint
of the irrigation service particularly with respect to land preparation and
transplanting of paddy in the wet season; (iv) the optimum time for wheat
sowing in the dry season; and (v) the socio-economic potential of the farmers
served. Under the project, in the kharif season, paddy would continue to be
the dominant crop. With improved water distribution and regulation, it is
assumed that the area cultivated with local paddy varieties would decline
while the area cropped with BYV paddy would increase. For areas served by
project works, average cropping intensity at full development (Table T-5) has
been estimated at about 200% as compared with about 110X at present.
Crop Yields
6.05    Present yields of all crops cultivated by most farmers to be served
under the project are low because of the random and unreliable rainfall and a
justifiable reluctance to invest in modern inputs. Farmers follow rainfed
agricultural practices and tend to use local varieties even if they are able
to purchase water from private irrigation sources to deal with critical
drought periods. Applications of fertilizer and plant protection chemicals
are limited primarily because of cash resources and the heavy risks involved.
As a result, all rainfed (and even irrigated) crops produce low yields. No
increase in HYV paddy and wheat yields would be possible in the 'future
without' situation since farmers are unlikely to improve the quality of seeds
or apply improved methods of cultivation. In the 'future with' project
situation, yields of HYV paddy are estimated to reach 3.0 to 4.0 ton/ha
depending on the crop season, local paddy varieties 2.2 ton/ha; wheat
2.2 ton/ha; sesame 1.5 ton/ha; and oilseeds 1.0 ton/ha. The yield of
potatoes is estimated to be about 20 ton/ha. These irrigated crop yields
reflect efficient water regulation, and moderate improvements compared to
present irrigated agricultural practises with respect to land preparation,
use of fertilizer, quality of seed used, weed and pest control, cultivation



-38-
and planting techniques, and timeliness of harvest and threshing. The-crop
yields also assume the degree of under irrigation presently practiced in WB
on holdings with private irrigation sources and a moderately effective
agricultural extension service. Estimates of crop yields and production are
summarized in Table T-1l.
Input Requirements
6.06    Present use of fertilizer and plant protection chemicals by the
majority of potential project beneficiaries is minimal and is mostly limited
to paddy, wheat and maize. Even farmers with their own irrigation sources
and the potential to apply full crop water requirements use only about a
third of the recommended chemical inputs. Very few farmers use good quality
seeds (paddy, wheat and maize); most farmers meet their seed requirements
from their previous harvests. The average farmer owns one pair of draft
animals which provide adequate farm power for the small size holdings and
general farming practices.
6.07    Present and projected input requirements are presented in Annex 6,
SDV and reflect the low income group being reached by the project. Crop
yields used in the project analysis correspond with the assumed moderate
input levels. On average, existing facilities would be sufficient to meet
the small projected increase in input demand over present statewide consump-
tion but there may be local shortfalls in some areas once project tubewell
clusters become fully operational.
6.08    Future requirements of fertilizers and pesticides will depend on
availability rather than marketing and distribution. Since the declared (and
to date successfully pursued) policy of GOI is to bring on stream new fer-
tilizer plants and to increase importation of fertilizer and pesticide
materials not locally manufactured, the project input requirements shouid be
secure. The additional fodder and crop by-products under the project will
ensure that animal draft pover will continue to be adequate for the project's
needs. No labor shortages are expected in the project command areas, since
the project works will be scattered throughout the State, and there is con-
siderable unemployment in the rural areas.
Market ProsDects and Prices (Annex 2)
6.09    The project area covers most of WE and therefore the potential market
is large. Production increase, although substantial percentage-wise and in
terms of the immediate areas developed, is small in relation to the market.
Marketing of surplus produce is not expected to pose any significant problem
since the established trading system through private dealers and Government
markets would take care of disposing the surplus. Many small farmers mav
continue to receive unfavorable prices because they sell crops at harvest
time for want of suitable storage and in order to repay crop loans. It is
envisaged that the project would generate the need for more storage capacity
at local cooperatives to allow farmers to store their produce for some time
and thus benefit from higher seasonal prices.



-39-
6.10    No major changes in the relative crop prices are foreseen because
prices are largely determined by seasonal suppLy and demand pattern. The
Government's price supports for rice and wheat have been particularly effec-
tive in maintaining sufficient incentive for farmers, while keeping the real
price to consumers fairly stable. It has therefore been assumed that future
farmgate prices (in constant terms) for paddy and wheat would follow the
official procurement prices in 1984 of about Rs. 1800/ton and Rs. 1750/ton
for paddy and wheat respectively. Jute would continue to follow
well-established farmgate prices in the areas where it is the major crop
(about Rs. 2,250/ton in 1984). Prices for potatoes, vegetables and other
minor crops would follow market trends depending on location and on produc-
tion and demand in any year.
VII. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
General
7.01    Project works will be sited where most beneficiaries are farmers with
small holdings equal or less than 1.0 ha who would not normally be able to
develop an irrigation source at their own expense. In addition, the project
will cover most of WB and thus individual command areas will have a wide
range of climatic, soil and agronomic conditions throughout the State. There-
fore, in assessing the project's impact on typical farmers' incomes, it is
assumed that: (i) soil type can vary within the command area of individual
works; (ii) a farm will be about 1.0 ha with a number of separate parcels of
the same soil type; and (iii) the holding would at present be non-irrigated.
The main characteristics have been generated from socio-economic surveys and
data obtained from other minor irrigation development in WB supported by the
Bank Group (para 1.16) with further evidence provided by GOWB staff. The
detailed assumptions and crop budgets used in formulating model farm budgets
are given in Annex 2.
7.02    It is assumed that all types of tubewells would provide an irrigation
service of the same quantity and quality. With respect to RLIs, the comple-
tion of the distribution systems and conversion to electric powered pumps
would double the command area and improve the quality and reliability of the
irrigation service within the existing irrigated command of the RLI. The
ODWs under the project are located in areas where farm holdings will be
smaller then 1.0 ha and fragmented and beneficiaries will be mainly from
scheduled castes and tribes. The project provides for construction of wells
in both hard rock and alluvial areas designed so that they can be mechanized
by use of a small mobile pump set. To assess the impact of ODWs, it is
assumed that for manually operated open wells, limited water supplies would
restrict farmers to growing a small area (0.25 ha) of high value cash crops
(potatoes or vegetables). An assessment has also been made of the impact
when typical ODWs are mechanized; in this assessment, the pumping cost has
been treated as a farm input with an agricultural production impact as for
tubewells on medium soil types.
Farm Incomes
7.03    Average farm incomes from crop production before payment of water
charges assume that most farmers will move from rainfed to full irrigated
agriculture under the project. It is also assumed that without the project,



-40-
these farmers could not afford their own irrigation sources and thus their
real incomes would continue to stagnate at present levels. Even though, they
may be able to 'rent' water from private tubewell owners to protect their
crops under drought conditions, it is assumed that the limited irrigation
service would have only the marginal effect of making .'rainfed' crop yields
more reliable.
7.04    With the above assumptions, three model farm budgets for heavy,
medium and light soil types have been examined to assess the impact of
irrigation on farm incomes. It is expected that improvements in farm incomes
from crop production for actual farms would vary considerably depending on
location within the State. However, the farm location within a tubewell
command will not affect the quality of irrigation service. The likely impact
for the representative farms is summarized in Table T-13 and detailed in
Annex 2 (Tables 3 to 6). The increase in farm incomes from crop production
before payment of water charges would average about 170X for project works
other than ODWs, about 220% for ODWs without pumpsets and about 250X for
mechanized ODWs. Total family income would rise only about 140% since it
includes income from other sources which is assumed to remain constant.
Imuact on Poverty
7.05    For farmers without a private irrigation source in the project area,
annual incomes per capita 'with' and .'without' the project are estimated as
follows:

This compares to an estimated average State per capita income of about
Rs 1,680. Thus none of the farm types presently reacb the State average
income level, although individual farms within project command areas may
exceed it. Based on a minimum caloric requirement of 2,100 Kcal. per capita
worth of about Rs 740 at present farmgate prices, and other minimum consump-
tion requirements of about Rs 260 per capita, the poverty line for project
impact areas can be taken as Rs 1,000 per zapita. On these assumptions, at
present, most farm households and almost all the farming population live
below this poverty line.
7.06    At full development, for farmers served by RLIs and tubewells the
average per capita income before water charges would increase by about
Rs 690. However, all farms would not benefit equally from this increase.
Only farming units above about 0.70 ha would exceed the poverty level. Thus,
even assuming all farmers could obtain predicted cropping intensities and
yields at full development, about 50% of the farming population cultivating
20% of the land within a typical project tubewell command would still live
under poverty conditions.  For farmers served by unmechanized ODWs, average



-41-
income would increase by Rs 650/ha. Only farmers with ODWs growing high
vaue cash crops with an easily accessible market would exceed the poverty
level.
7.07    A primary project concern will be to assist farmers to obtain the
other inputs necessary to generate the incremental crop production possible
with reliable irrigation; in particular, those farmers who may be unable to
generate a reliable income from crop production to sustain their families and
provide sufficient cash flow to buy inputs for modern irrigated agriculture.
It appears that for most small and marginal farmers (land owners or tenants),
either farming is a secondary income to other activities or they rent addi-
tional land which can be developed as envisaged under the project. The
analysis assumes that some small farmers grow crops for home consumption
utilizing project facilities to the extent that their cash budget allows.
Thus, in terms of formulating a cost recovery policy and appropriate water
charges, consideration must be given to the weaker elements of the project
population; that is those farm families that may remain below the poverty
line even with full development benefits. To achieve this objective, GOIVB
would carry out a study of farmers benefitting from the project. The study
would be undertaken by the M&E Unit of the AED (para 5.26) assisted by the
DOA and would: (i) evaluate the extent that weaker elements of the project
population are able to fully utilize the irrigation service and to purchase
other inputs necessary to achieve full development benefits; and (ii) formu-
late proposals to enable the full development benefits of the project to be
achieved in a timely manner.
Capital and O&M Costs
7.08    For purposes of cost recovery, the estimated project costs have been
adjusted and separated into two parts. Fixed Costs include the capital cost
of constructing the project irrigation works and costs for those items (such
as pump units) which need to be replaced after some time; they also include
fixed O&H Costs which do not vary appreciably with tubewell operating hours.
The only variable cost is electricity which is a function of pump unit
utilization. Annual costs including assumptions of annual operating hours
for typical project irrigation works are detailed in Table T-12 and are
summarized as follows:

Ideally, and if full cost recovery were the target, farmers would be charged

Repayment CaDacity of Farmers
7.09    For each farm model (para 7.05), the incremental land rent or project
rent has been estimated. It is defined as incremental income less allowances
for depreciation, use of own resources (family labor and bullock power),
perceived risk and own management services; it thus measures the net incen-
tive of farmers to participate in the project. If the entire project rent
(if known) was taxed away, this would leave a farmer indifferent to par-
ticipating in the project. With assumptions used in the analysis, the
project rent does not exhibit significant variations among the areas with
three main soil types. The estimates of project rent for each type of farm
model are shown in Table T-13. The project rent is calculated for a 'typi-
cal' farming situation and can only be estimated within broad tolerances.
However, as most of the beneficiaries would be small or marginal farmers, it
may not be desirable that a substantial portion of the project rent is made
available for cost recovery.
Present Cost Recoverv
7.10    Background.  The policy of GOWB is to tax the agricultural sector
with due recognition of low income levels in the rural areas. With respect
to minor irrigation, the stated policy objective of GOWB is to provide all
farmers with strong incentives to utilize irrigation in order to benefit the
State through increased crop yields, productivity and increased employment in
rural areas. With the exception of cash crops, surplus incremental produc-
tion reaching the market will be limited, since siting criteria will ensure
that farm holdings reached under the project will be small. Thus, the GOWB
cannot rely on the project generating much revenue through indirect agricul-
ture taxation such as sales tax and the main source of cost recovery from
project beneficiaries with present taxation policies will be water charges.
7.11    Present Water Charges.  In general, the State has made considerable
efforts to rationalize cost recovery from public minor irrigation schemes.
The Government regularly reviews charges and presently has a different level
of charges for kharif and rabi seasons to encourage a greater utilization of
the irrigation service. The State has introduced legislation to charge for
water on a volumetric basis but since the quantity of water delivered cannot
be measured with existing technology, a volumetric water rate is not practi-
cal. Consequently, a notional water requirement for each of the main crops
has been assumed so that the volumetric water charge can be applied on the
irrigated area for each crop. In 1984, the base voAumetric charge was raised
from Rs 1.6 to 5.0 per acre-inch (Rs 0.02 to 0.05/m ) for DTWs and RLIs
resulting in a substantial increase in revenue. Furthermore, the payment
procedure is such that farmers must pay water charges before the crop season
begins thus ensuring ivO0 recovery of assessed water charges from land to be
irrigated.
)j For WB, the long-range marginal cost of power is about Rs 0.85-0.90/kWh
at the sub-station or about Rs 1.00/kWh at the individual tubewell making
allowance for transmission losses.



-43-
7.12    For typical project works which are fully utilized, and with effec-
tive and efficient management, it is estimated that in an average rainfall
year, revenue would cover average O&M costs with the present level of water
charges. However, for existing vorks managed by the AED, water charges do
not cover the O&M charges for three main reasons:
(i)  existing works do not provide reliable, timely and equitable irriga-
tion service and incomplete distribution systems mean that actual
irrigated area is considerably less than potential;
(ii)  O&M costs are excessive because of overmanning particularly at the
operator level; and
(iii)  accounting procedures vithin the AED do not enable management to
pinpoint ineffective and inefficient O&M practices.
In addition to the technical and management problems, all of which are
addressed by the project, the cost recovery system presently used in IB does
not fully take into consideration physical factors which affect the value
of irrigation service provided. First, there are large annual and seasonal
variations in demand and large differences in water requirements for the main
crops depending on climatic/soil conditions. Thus, a system of charges based
on unit irrigated area results in a random rate depending on location and,
more important, encourages farmers to take more water than they need to the
detriment of those further away from the water source. Second, the quality
of the irrigation service provided varies considerably throughout the State,
and since water charges are uniform statewide, the level of charges has to
take into account the value of the poorest service offered.
Cost Recovery under the Project
7.13    The improved technology of new DTWs and modernized RLIs would guaran-
tee reliable, timely and equitable irrigation service. Furthermore, a basic
project objective is to make management improvements to reduce or eliminate
the causes of poor performance and low efficiency of public minor irrigation
in the State. For RLIs and DTWs of improved design under the project, the
quantity of water supplied at the well point would be the same as that reach-
ing the fields because conveyance losses are near zero. This means that
there is a major difference between the service from improved and old stand-
ard minor irrigation systems not only in the quantity of water that each
farmer receives but also in the quality of the irrigation service. In addi-
tion, improved minor irrigation technology would provide an 'on demand'
irrigation service (since the farmer decides when to open his outlet) which
can be charged on a volumetric basis such that revenue is proportional to the
tubewell operating hours. A problem for GOWB is how to charge for access to
a reliable irrigation service in addition to some charge which reflects use
of that service, particularly when project works will be randomly distributed
amongst existing works designed to traditional standards
7.14    The primary concern of the GOWB is that minor irrigation development
should not be a financial burden on the State. On the other hand, the cost
recovery system and level of water charges should provide sufficient incen-
tive to encourage all farmers to fully utilize the irrigation service (par-
ticularly in the kharif season). Thus in formulating an cost recovery
policy, the GOWB will consider three separate but interrelated elements:



-44-
(i) financial monitoring to enable 0J costs and revenue from water charges
to be compared; (ii) an appropriate cost recovery system; and (iii) the level
of water charges.
7.15    Financial Monitoring.  To enable the project management to finan-
cially monitor O&k activities, the existing accounting procedures in the AED
need to be strengthened; this will be undertaken as part of the AED Manage-
ment Study (para 5.10). A priority concern, vill be to collect data to
support the O&M budget request to enable minor irrigation works to be effec-
tively and efficiently operated and maintained such that predicted benefits
can be achieved. In this respect, establishment, energy and maintenance
costs should be accounted for under separate headings. In addition, account-
ing procedures should enable O&M costs for existing new works to be separated
so that costs can be analysed. This will enable GOIvB to establish what
portion of O&M costs are due to inefficiencies in management and/or technical
limitations; the GOWB would then have data to decide how much beneficiaries
are paying for these inefficiencies. Accounts should also be kept in such a
way that GOWB/AED can identify costs of managing a typical installation of
each type of technology as well as the overall minor irrigation program.
With these considerations, the AED will have a basis for setting realistic
0&M cost allocations for the various existing and new works. Finally, the
AED should collect revenue data from water charges on a seasonal basis so
that revenue can be related to W X costs for individual works and by
organizational work units.
7.16    Water Char2e System.  For reasons explained above (para 7.12), an
appropriate water charge system for project works could be different from
that for existing minor irrigation works. The improved technology and water
management arrangements would enable vater charge system for project works to
be based on 'hours of use'. Such a volumetric charge system would be basi-
cally different from the present statewide water charge system (although
implicit in the existing legislation); the practicality of introducing a new
system would have to be checked and cleared at appropriate levels of Govern-
ment. The cost recovery system should provide the opportunity for incentives
to encourage farmers to fully utilize the irrigation service and take account
of repayment capacity of the target group of beneficiaries. To achieve these
objectives, the GOIB would consider a two (or more) tier water charge.
First, there should be a base charge for the service which reflects the
.'opportunity cost' of the service. For improved tubewells and BLIs under the
project, this charge could relate to the average costs of works which enable
all users to have an equal quality of service and a greater quantity of vater
because losses are reduced. Already, the State has legislation to levy a
'betterment' charge which could be used as the proposed base charge. Second,
there should be a volumetric water charge which would provide revenue that is
proportional to tubewell utilization. Since there is insufficient informa-
tion at present to enable GOWB to introduce such a .'tiered' water charge
system, the State will undertake a study to formulate an appropriate policy
and agree a timetable for introducing such a policy; in particular, the study
would take into account the efficiency and reliability of the irrigaticn
service as well as the quantity of water provided. With respect to STWs, it
is expected that charges based on 'hours of operation' vill be adopted when
STWs are finally handed over to panchayat management (para 5.10). Until the
STWs are handed over to the panchayats, beneficiaries would pay the same
water charges as for DTWs and BLIs.



-45-
7.17    For reasons outlined above, agreements have been reached with GOWB
that it would: (i) by December 31, 1985, prepare and submit to IDA for
review, draft terms of reference for a water charge system study, and taking
into account IDA's comments, carry out the study; (ii) furnish to IDA for its
review and comment, not later than December 31, 1986, the results of the
study including a proposal for establishing a water charge system for
irrigated areas served by project works and by existing minor irrigation
works; (iii) taking into account IDA's comments on this proposal, furnish
to IDA not later than September 30, 1987, for its review and comment a
timetable for applying the new water charge system; and (iv) taking into
account IDA-s comments, implement the new water charge system in accordance
with this timetable within the project area.
7.18    Proiected Level of Cost Recovery.  For existing minor irrigation
works, the O&H budget allocations for minor irrigation works are not directly
dependent on cost recovery. This is fortunate because it would mean that
farmers would have to pay for any technical or institutional inefficiencies
that result in low utilization of public tubewells or RLIs. Under the cir-
cumstances, present water charges for existing DTWs and RLIs in WB may be
more than adequate for the quality of service provided. Nonetheless, to
ensure that minor irrigation development under the project does not become a
burden on Government, agreements have been reached with GOWB that it would:
(i) continue to set water charges on existing and new irrigation systems with
regular reviews of the cost of operation and maintenance; and (ii) for new
and modernized irrigation schemes in the project area, ensure that revenue
covers the average operation and maintenance costs and a reasonable share of
investment costs, taking into account the repayment capacity of irrigation
beneficiaries.
7.19    Given the present income levels of most project beneficiaries, the
immediate concern of GOWB with respect to cost recovery levels would be to
Price for full utilization of the irrigation service. Only if small or
marginal farmers can achieve full development production is there a
reasonable chance of progressive increases in water charges above current
levels.
7.20    Since the project structure and exact level of water charges which
would be applicable at full development cannot be predicted at this time,
revenue projections for project works have been based on present water rates
which may represent the variable' portion of a two-tier water charge system,
when such a system is adopted (para 7.16). The present values of recovery
thus projected are given in Table T-13. The average rent recovery index
would be about 35Z. The average cost recovery is estimated to be about 42X.
While it may be conceptually feasible to charge higher water rates to project
beneficiaries, the scope for achieving this is limited. Income levels of
most farm families at full development under the project will remain close to
the poverty level and it may not be practical to have a differential water
rate that reflects farm income levels. Per capita income after water charges
would average about Rs 1,300. An estimated 50% of farm households would
remain below the projected poverty threshold of US$90 per capita compared to
about 95% without the project.



-46-
VIII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
General
8.01    The provision of a reliable public minor irrigation service would
substantially reduce farmers' risk and produce incentives for complementary
investments such as higher input applications and adoption of advanced farm-
ing practices. It would also lead to a more efficient utilization and
management of groundwater and energy in the public groundwater sector.
Moreover, the improved technology would provide an important social benefit
in that, for the first time in public irrigation development in WB, there can
be equity in water allocation. Project benefits would be generated as fol-
lows:
Works              Command Area           Farm
Type  No. of Units  Served (ha)    Families Served (No.)
(i)  DTis     3,400 Ia      67,000             96,000
(ii)  STWs     5,400         32,000             46,000
(iii)  ODWs    10,000         24,000             25,000
5iv)  RLIs       200         16.000 /b          23,000
Total   19,000        139,000            190,000
/a High, Medium and Low capacity DTWs (para 3.06).
/b Including about 8,000 ha served by existing RLIs before modernization.
In addition, considerable indirect benefits, which cannot be quantified, will
be generated through the management improvements under the project.
8.02    The overall impact of the project in terms of increased agricultural
production and employment generated at full development is snmmarized below:
Increased foodgrain production (tons/year)             367,000
Increased potato production (tons/year)                140,000
Increased jute production (tons/year)                   18,000
Increased sesame production (tons/year)                  5,000
Value added to the local economy (Rs M/year)               5?d
Generated farm employment (iobs)                       180.000
8.03    Production Benefits.  The project would, increase foodgrain produc-
tion by about 367,000 tons, equivalent to an increase of about 130Z over
production without the project. Incremental rice and wheat production would
be mainly available for on-farm consumption for small and marginal farmers;
any surpluses would be marketed within the State. Increased maize production
would be available locally where it is a staple crop. Additional production
of pulses would help alleviate a growing statewide scarcity in that it is the
main source of pxatein for human consumption. Jute and potato production
would be marketed as cash crops.
8.04    Employment.  It is estimated that labor requirements in agriculture
would approximately triple as a result of the project from about 23 M to
about 70 N man-days per year at full development. Most of this labor would
be needed during the rabi (the months of labor surplus in rural areas), since
without irrigation, most land remains fallow. The increase would be due to



-47-
adoption of irrigation, increases in cropped area, more intensive care of
crops and the higher labor needs for harvesting. The labor requirements
would be reflected mainly in the reduction of the present high level of
unemployment and underemployment of family labor, particularly on the smaller
farms. Agricultural laborers and marginal farmers would benefit from
increased labor demand on larger farms and from non-farm employment oppor-
tunities generated by the project due to increased demands for marketing,
transport and processing facilities. About 20,000 jobs for unskilled labor
would be created mainly for constructing the project works. The project
establishment would create permanent jobs for about 2,900 tubewell operators.
8.05    Beneficiares.  The project would benefit about 190,000 farm families
by project completion and indirectly a large number of landless laborers that
has not been estimated. Most beneficiaries would be farmers who cannot
afford a private irrigation source and are unlikely to be eligible for long
term credit. The project will thus help overcome existing and potential
inequities in the use of the key resources of groundwater and irrigable land.
However, even though the project provides the opportunity to utilize an
efficient irrigation to all farmers served, some marginal farmers may not
initially be able to afford the necessary cash inputs to fully benefit from
irrigation. The cash income and/or favorable credit conditions may become
important factors in effective participation of small farmers in the project
and would be carefully monitored (para 7.07).
Economic Analysis
8.06    The project consists of a large number of individual components of
various types of minor irrigation development. Thus, typical examples of
each individual component (para 3.06) represent the project objectives to be
tested; the overall ERR of the project (about 37Z) has limited significance.
8.07    Only agricultu-al production benefits have been entered in the
economic analysis. Economic prices for tradable crops (rice, wheat, jute)
were derived from relevant border prices and for non-traded crops (pulses,
oilseeds, vegetables) from the prevailing and projected market prices. The
economic prices of fertilizers were derived from IBRD price projections. The
shadow price of agricultural labor was assumed at about 50% of the going wage
rates for hired labor. Cost items were valued by employing specific conver-
sion factors to financial costs.  Benefit build-up is the result of the
assumed phasing of areas to come under irrigation, a four year transition
from present to future cropping intensities and a more gradual build-up of
yields, input use, and adoption ox high yielding crop varieties. Full
project development within a tubewell or RLI command area would be reached
after about six years. The cost of pumping has been based on the estimated
marginal cost of electricity for WB. Detailed assumptions on economic
analysis are given in Annex 2.
8.08    The economic cost of all civil works, equipment, land acquisition,
administration and engineering, operation and maintenance have been included
in the derivation of the ERR. Discounting costs and benefits o"-.r a 30-year
period (at 12% discount rate), the estimated ERR and the net present value at
12% discount rate for the various project components are as followu:
