and benefit streams for the analysis for typical project components are shown
in Table T-14; the cost and benefits streams for the remaining project com-
ponents are given in Annex 2. Project viability can also be expressed in
terms of cost and benefits per unit of water. The costs of producing and
delivering water to the plant for a typical project facility ranges from
about 0.23 Rslm3 for STW unit (clustered) to about 0.35 Rs/m3for a typical
MTW; this compares to the implicit value of water at the plant of about
0.60 Rs/m3 at full development.
Sensitivity Analysis
8.09    Several tests were performed to evaluate the sensitivity of assump-
tions made in the economic analysis. One test was calculation of the switch-
ing value for the various assumptions to assess what change in the variable
would reduce the discounted present value of the project's net incremental
benefits to zero. The discount rate used was 12% which has been taken as the
estimated opportunity cost of capital in India. The results of the main
variables tested for a typical high capacity DTW are as follows:
Appraisal       Switching
Variable Tested                           Value           Value          Change %
Number of Running Hours                     1,800 /a           900 /b       -50
Net Incremental Crop Production (Rs'000)  1,475               549           -63
Construction Costs (Rs'O0O)                   343           1,268           270
0&M Costs (Rs '000)                           207           1,132           448
/a  16 hours per day operation during the peak irrigation period.
lb 8 hours per day operation during the peak irrigation period.
Sensitivity tests using the same variables were made for the other project
irrigation components; the analysis is given in Annex 2 and shows similar
results. The tests indicate that only large changes (and unlikely in the



-49-
magnitude required) in the main assumptions will turn this project economi-
cally unviable. For example, the test with respect to net crop production
indicates that the project-s NPV would reduce to zero if farmers fully util-
ized the irrigat-onn service on only about 40X of the net command area of a
typical HDTW.
8.10    Proiect Risk.  The project risks are less than would normally be
expected with an operation of this type because lessons learnt from the UP
Public Tubewell Projects (para 3.05) have helped to identify and minimize the
potential risks involved. The major problem with RLIs and DTWs in the State
has been incomplete distribution systems and management constraints which
result from the existing designs. Another possible risk to project benefits
would be underutilization of minor irrigation systems. Apparent lack of
demand for water with existing public minor irrigation works is primarily due
to the inadequate quantity and quality of the irrigation service. Proven
designs of the works under the project are such that all project irrigation
systems would be able to provide a reliable, timely and equitable service.
Thus, underutilization of project wells is unlikely to be due to the technol-
ogy but rather due to lack of farmers response to an assured irrigation
service, particularly in the kharif season when farmers save water cbarges
in anticipation of good monsoon rainfall. The project provides for a com-
prehensive monitoring and study of the water charge system so that action
can be taken if necessary to minimize this risk. Particular attention would
be given to small and marginal farmers who may have casb flow problems that
could reduce their ability to invest in other agricultural inputs such as
fertilizers, or even commit themselves to expenditure on water charges.
Environmental Effects
8.11    The project areas have been under wet season cultivation for many
decades and introduction of irrigation improvements in the dry season are not
expected to produce a significant change in environmental conditions. Water
from tne project wells is potable and is expected to have a health benefit by
reduction of the occurrence of intestinal diseases. The water delivery
through buried pipes is expected to reduce the potential hazards of malaria
associated with some surface irrigation schemes. Nonetheless, an agreement
has been reached with GOWB that it would take all measures as may be con-
sidered necessary to minimize the risk of malaria, filaria and other
water-related diseases within the project area.

INDIA
WEST BENGAL MINOR IRRIGATION PROJECT
Assumptions for Economic and Financial Analysis
Price and Conversion Factors
1.      General.  The economic prices of major traded agricultural com-
modities (at farm gate) were derived from the IBRD world market prices (1984
projected through 1995) expressed in 1984 currency values with appropriate
adjustments for freight, handling and processing.  Non-traded foodgrains and
other non-traded goods and services were brought to a common basis with the
traded goods and services through the uEa of conversion factors. Three
conversion factors have been used: (i) Standard Conversion Factor (SCF); (ii)
Construction Conversion Factor (CCF); and (iii) Food Conversion Factor (FCF).
Details on the derivation of the conversion factors and their use in
described below. The opportunity cost of capital (OCC) was assumed at 12Z.
A standard exchange rate of US$1.0 to Rs 11.0 was used to convert prices
expressed in US do!lars to local currency values. The assumptions used to
derive the estimates are described below.
2.      Standard Conversion Factor.  For economic analysis, costs and
benefits of the project have been evaluated at world market prices in order
to measure India's net economic benefits from the project. This requires
certain price adjustments, since many items are not traded in the world
markets and the prices of others are affected by tariffs and trade restric-
tions. To make them comparatle, a SCF have been applied to the price of
non-traded goods. In the absence of trade restrictions, the SCF can be
estimated by using the following formula:
SCF  =         X + M
X + S  + M + TM
where: X      export value (f.o.b.);
Y  =  import  alue (c.i.f.);
2 -  subsidies on exports;
T  =  taxes and duties on imports
For the period since 1970, this calculation has produced a SCF of 0.86.
ho-ever, since this method does not take into consideration quantitative
restrictions on trade, tbe estimates derived provide only an upper limit for
the SCF. The Indian Planning Commission recommends to use in the evaluation
of industrial projects, conversion factors for foreign exchange and untraded
goods that imply a SCF of about 0.75. This estimate is considered to be
nearer to the lower limit of the real SCF. Consequently, a SCF of 0.8 has
been used in the economic analysis.



ANNEX 2
-76-                        Page  2
3.      Construction Ccnversion Factor.  The construction of project civil
works require the use of a mixture of equipment, materials, and skilled and
unskilled labor, the proportions of which vary with the type of minor irriga-
tion installation. The conversion factor of 1.00 has been used for works
which require imported materials; domestically manufactured equipment,
engineering and administration charges and skilled labor requirements have
been adjusted by the SCF (0.80). The share of unskilled labor has been
adjusted by the conversion factor of 0.50 (para 6). Based on this analysis,
the following CCF have been used in the economic analysis to convert the
financial into economic prices:
TyRe of Installation                             CCF
HDTW, MDTW and RLI                               0.82
LDTW and STW                                     0.79
ODW (Hard rock)                                  0.63
ODW (Alluvium)                                   0.71
4.      Econcmic Prices of Commodities and Foodgrain Conversion Factor (FCF).
The economic prices for traded commodities such as rice, wheat, groundnuts
and jute were derived by using border prices in US dollars according to IBRD
commodity projections and converted to local currency values at the standard
exchange rate. The domestic cost components (such as local transport, han-
dling and marketing charges) were adjusted by a SCF of 0.80 (Table 1). The
economic prices for non-traded foodgrain (pulses and gram were assumed to be
non-traded) were derived by applying a FCF gj to the present financial
prices. The recent downward revisions of IBRD agricultural commodity price
forecasts nave brought the world market price of wheat and rice to about
parity with the domestic market prices in India. As a result, a FCF of 1.0
has been used in the economic analysis.
5.      Prices of Agricultural InDuts.  Fertilizers have been valued at the
projected world market price (Table 1). Pesticides and insecticides have
been assumed to be traded. In order to make an allowance for taxes and
subsidies and for the cost of transport and marketing, a conversion factor
of 0.95 has been applied to their financial prices. The economic price of
seed and miscellaneous charges has been assumed to approximate their finan-
cial price. The economic price of farm power (bullock pair with operator)
was derived from going daily rental rates, adjusted by the SCF. A summary of
economic and financial prices is presented in Table 2.
6.      Opportunity Cost of Labor.  The rural labor market in the State of WB
belongs to the most depressed in India mainly due to heavy population pres-
sures and the lack of employment opportunities particularly in the slack
periods. The incremental demand for labor generated by construction of civil
works and later on by irrigated agriculture would be easily absorbed by the
existing labor force without any noticable impact on present wage rates.
Analysis of farm labor supply and demand in various Indian irrigation
projects typically give an economic shadow wage rate for farm labor in the
jj Estimated as a rat o of wheat and rice production valued at economic
pricas to wheat and rice production valued at average domestic market
prices.



-77-
ANNEX 2
Page 3
range of 1/2 to 2/3 of the average financial wage rate. The current average
wage rate in rural areas amounts to 8.0 Rs/day. Given the exceptionally
depressed rural labor market in WB the conversion factor of 0.50 has been
assumed for conversion of the average financial into economic wage rate.
ARricultural Benefits
7.      Present Situation.  The present level of agricultural production is
described in Annex 5, SDV. The level of agricultural development varies with
agro-climatic and market conditions across the State. Between 1970/71 and
1981/82, agricultural production in WB remained virtually stagnant with the
exception of potatoes and oilseeds. Assessment of yields, input levels and
cropping patterns were based on Ci) direct information in the field, (ii)
district statistics, (iii) discussions with local agricultural staff and
farmers and (iv) GOWB surveys and evaluation studies. The soil type (heavy,
medium and light soils) has been selected as the critical variable in the
analysis. Other important factors such as land distribution and fragmenta-
tion pattern, rainfall are relatively uniform throughout the State. The
exception to this general pattern are areas designated for dugwells in the
western part of the State.
8.      Future Without Proiect.  Without irrigation facilities no substantial
changes from the present situation would result. For the purpose of the
project analysis, it has been assumed that there will be no private irriga-
tion sources within the project command areas, and that it would be unlikely
that private irrigation facilities would develop without the project.
9.      Future With Project.  The transition of agriculture from the
'present" to the ."future with." project situations was based on the following
assumptions:
(a)  Yields. Inputs and Economic Prices.  A six-year build-up period
for present to future improved cropping practices has been
assumed. For simplicity, the same period has been taken for the
build-up of crop yields and inputs, as well as for the effect of
changing ecQnomic prices on net returns per ha of each crop.
The assumed pattern foc each year after the start of irrigation
is presented below.
(b) Cropping Intersities. Experience from other similar irrigation
projects suggests that increases in cropping intensity to the full
development level would be faster than increases in yields. A
period of four years was assumed for the transition from present
to projected intensities. The rate is shown below:
Year
Rates of Chanre             1        2       3                 5        6
Yields, inputs, economic  20        50      70      85        95       100
prices
Cropping intensities       30      65       85     100       100       100



ANNEX 2
-78-                          Page  4
The transition of agriculture practices in the RLI commands to be modernized
under the project was assumed to taken half the time expected for the
tubewells since the farmers are more likely to practice irrigated agriculture
in the :pre-project" situation.
Costs
10.     CaRital Costs.  The capital cost estimates were based on September,
1984 unit prices. They were adjusted by specific construction conversion
factors (para 3). The project was evaluated over a 30-year period. The
effective life of wells and the buried pipe distribution systems were assumed
as 30 years and that of pumpsets as 10 years. These assumptions are based on
the experience in India of operating wells and distribution systems similar
to the project design. The replacement cost of the pumpsets has been taken
into account.
11.     Land Acquisition.  The buried pipe distribution system would limit
land acquisition requirements to the area required for siting the water point
(about 0.10 ha per DTW). The land lost in this way to agricultural produc-
tion was valued at the economic net return per ha without the project. The
net return foregone each year (Rs 300 per DTW) was treated as a recurrent
project cost and was subtracted from the 'with project' benefits.
12.     Cost of Pumpin2.  The electric power supply and demand characteris-
tics of WB are described in Annex 8, SDV. Based on recent incremental cost
studies of power generation and transmission in the State, the marginal rate
of Rs 1.00 per kWh has been adopted in the project analysis. The build-up of
pumping cost has been assumed proportional to the build-up of cropping inten-
sities (para 9). The energy cost would vary with the type of irrigation
facility as follows:
(a) STW with centrifugal pump and electric motor operated for 1,800 hrs.
p.a. and delivering 20m3/h through a dynamic head of 8m. Energy
consumption is 0.96 kWh giving a total consumption of 1,728 kWh p.a.
The economic cost of energy at the end of the 0.4 kv line is Rs 1.0
kWh. Thus the annual economic cost of energy is Rs 1,900.
(b) LDTW with submersible electric pump operated for 1,800 hrs. p.a.
and delivering 20m3/h through a dynamic head of 12m. Energy consump-
tion is 1.44 kWh giving a total consumption of 2,592 kWh p.a. The
economic cost of energy at the end of the 0.4 kv line is Rs 1.1 per
kWh.  Thus the annual economic cost of energy is Rs 2,851.
(c)  NDTW with submersible electric pump operated for 1,800 hrs.  p.a.
and delivering 100m.3/h through a dynamic head of 12m. Energy consump-
tion is 5.6 kWh giving a total consumption of 10,080 kWh p.a. The
economic cost of energy at the end of the 11/0.4 kv transformer is
taken as Rs 1.0 kWh. Thus the annual economic cost of energy is Rs
10,080.
(d)  EDrW with submersible electric pump operated for 1,800 hrs.  p.a. and
delivering 200m3/h through a dynamic head of 15m. Energy consumDtion
is 13.9 kWh giving a total consumption of 23,400 kWh p.a. The
economic cost of energy at the 11/0.4 transformer in .aken as Rs 1.0
per kWh. Thus the annual economic cost of energy is Rs 23,400.



ANNEX 2
-79_                            Page  5
13.     Operation and Maintenance Costs.  It has been assumed that annual
maintenance charges would be equal to 1% of capital cost for dug vells and
drilled wells of all types, pump houses and piped conveyance systems. Submer-
sible pumps would require about 32 and centrifugal pumps about 5X of capital
costs for annual maintenance. Maintenance cost of power lines is implicit in
the electricity charges.
14.     Labor requirements for operation of project tubewells and RLI would
be substantially reduced compared to present norms due to automation.
Moreover, the level of skill would also be significantly reduced which would
enable to hire local village labor. The charges are summarized as follows:
(a)  LDTW in cluster of six vells with one operator/supervisor per cluster
at Rs 9,600 p.a. and one nigh guard per cluster at Rs 2,500 p.a. i.e.
about Rs 2,020 p.a. per well. Adjusted by the SCF of 0.80 for
economic analysis the rate per well is Rs 1,620 p.a.
(b)  MT  and HDTW with submersible electric motors and equipped for
automatic operation would require one record keeper at about Rs 4,000
p.a. Adjusted by the SCF of 0.80 the rate per well has been taken as
Rs 3,600 p.a.
Economic Analysis
15.     The Economic Rate of Return (ERR).  Based on the above assumptions,
the ERR and the Net Present Values (NPV) were estimated for the various
facilities proposed under the project as follows:

16.     Sensitivity Analysis.  The main assumptions in tne project analysis
have been tested by estimated the switching values as follows:

The reduction of rural power supply to about 8 hours per day during the peak
demand period (an unlikely event) would reduce the ERR to 12X. However, this
would be the case only if power is rationed orderly and farmers are notified
well in advance. Unexpected power supply variability would have a more
severe impact, since it could be assumed that the higher the unexpected
variability of power supply the lower the irrigation benefits i.e. the
switching value of 900 hours per day probably understates the impact on ERR.
This is because the farmers would receive an unreliable irrigation service
which would increase their risk aversion to irrigated agriculture and to the
purchase of the inputs necessary to achieve increased crop yields. The
sensitivity to net incremental crop production has been tested by calculating
the switching value of agricultural benefits with and without project. It is
assumed that lower crop yields would be a result of lower than the estimated
input costs required to achieve full crop yields with the project.
16.     In addition to the above analysis, the effect of the following
occurences on the ERR was tested:
(a) 0M Labor Requirements. Assuming that the project irrigation
facilities would be staffed according to present AED norms the ERR
for a typical DTW would decline by 2% points. However a far more
serious eff2ct of maintaining AED norms for project works would be
the financial impact on AED budget. The financial burden on the AED
budget would increase to about Rs 60 N annually rather then Rs 40 M
with modified staffing norms under the project.
(b) Kharif Benefits Excluded. The high rainfall in WB during the kharif
season makes the estimates of kharif benefits (supplementary irriga-
tion) difficult to quantify accurately. Moreover, it may be argued
that irrigation is not reqiired during the rainy season. The exclu-
sion of tbese benefits would reduce the ERR for a typical DEW to
about 32%. However, at the same time it is important to note that
DTW are most valuable in times of monsoon failure.
(c) Cluster Principle on STW and LDTW. The cost of these insts:lations
would significantly increase if not sited in c!:t-rs.  If sited
individually the ERR would decline to 44% and 331 for the STW and
LDTW respectively.
(d) Smaller Command Area for STW ar-i LDTW. It is expected that under the
project the comand areas served by STW and LDTW would be about 6 ha.
However, for various reasons, existing wells serve areas of



-81-
ANNEX 2
Page 7
about 4 ha. If the command area of the project units was taken as
4 ha, the ERR would decline to 35X and 262 for a typical STW and LDTIW
respectively.
(e) Future Without Proiect Benefits. It has been assumed that no sub-
stautial changes from the present situation would results in the
project area without irrigation. Alternatively, assuming a 3% com-
pounded annual growth rate of agriculture production in the "without
project:' situation over the next 10 years, the ERR for a typical DTW
would be reduced by about four percentage points.
(f) Mobile PumDs for ODW. The provision of mobile pump units under the
project would increase the conmand areas from about 0.25 ha to 2 ha
and 4 ha for dugvells in hard rock s=d alluvial areas respectively.
A mobile pump would typically serve a cluster of 3-5 dugwells. The
ERR for a mechanized dugwell would be about 40% and well over 50% in
hard rock and alluvial area respectively.
(g) The Opportunity Cost of Labor. The project would about triple the
demand for farm labor and as a result the opportunity cost of labor
may increase beyond the 4.0 Rs/day assumed in the analysis. With
the economic wage rate equal to the average financial wage rate of
8.0 Rs/day the ERR would decline by about six percentage points for
a typical DTW.

INDIA
WEST BENGAL MINOR IRRIGATION PROECT
Terms of Reference - AED  Management Study
Introduction
1.      Background.  The project introduces improved technology for DTWs and
RLIs which will make the irrigation service more efficient qnd, for the first
time in WB, provide minor irrigation systems vhich are predictably manage-
able. In addition, the project includes STWs and ODWs as public sector
operations under the AED, which expands the management responsibility of the
AED. The GOWB has recognized that when the existing technologies have been
substantially improved, the potential benefits ca:. only be fully obtained if
management can be made more effective and efficient. Since, the new tech-
nologies provide a predictable service, performance of management staff can
be evaluated and beneficiaries can assess whether they are getting the serv-
ice promised. In particular, management improvements can be structured to
focus on specific measures to set standards of responsibility and account-
ability and introduce incentives in the management procedures. Thus, GOWB
plans to: (i) strengthen the agencies responsible for execution and manage-
ment of the public minor irrigation works; (ii) establish procedures to use
village panchayats as ,management agents' for the shallow tubevell and open
dugwell components; and (iii) establish procedures to monitor progress of
project implementation and realization of project benefits.
2.      Already GOWB has made considerable progress in identifying areas
where the AED can be strengthened with most of recommendations coming from
senior staff within the organization. However, it is recognized that there
are limitations to the range of self-generated management improvements within
an organization. The GOWB and AED believe that there will be considerable
advantages in having independent consultants to assist the AED analyze its
management problems; the consultants would provide a group of experienced
multi-disciplinary professionals who are not burdened with routine
administrative duties and who can evaluate the organization and management
problems of the AED from an outside perspective.
3.      Assumptions.  With respect to AED management improvements the basic
objectives would be to: (i) improve the effectiveness and efficiency of the
existing AED administration; and (ii) ensure that project (and existing)
works can be managed to achieve predicted benefits. An assumed condition to
achieve these objectives would be that any change to the organization would
be such that the career prospects of existing AED staff are safeguarded.
Management Study
4.      The GOWB would retain the services of a Consultant Group experienced
in all aspects of management consultancy including financial management. In
addition to technical and management expertise, the Consultants would need to



Annex 4
-101-                      Page 2
be familiar with the practices and procedures of WB Government Departments,
and the minor irrigation sub-sector (including legislation related to land
and irrigation). The study would first undertake a preliminary review of the
AED organization structure and predicted workload over the project period.
This would be followed by an in-depth study of the main areas for possible
organizational and management improvements identified under the preliminary
review. The in-depth studies would result in a comprehensive report which
would detail practical improvements which can be made in West Bengal, and
recommends procedures and arrangements to implement such improvements. The
Consultants would have only a advisory role; responsibility for deciding what
would and should be done, and a timetable for making changes, would rest with
the AED/GOWB.
5.      The existing and proposea organization structure and staffing under
the project are summarized in para 5.04 of the World Bank Staff Appraisal
Report No. 5355-IN. The Management Study would review, evaluate and make
recommendations in the following main areas:
(a) Review the objectives and recommendations outlined in the project SAR
and SDV dealing with management and technical improvements under the
project and evaluate what changes can be made given constraints
inherent in existing organization structure, staffing levels, and
management procedures.
(b) Identify, review and evaluate changes required in the management and
organization structure of the AED to implement the construction
program for new works and undertake operation and maintenance of
existing works aDd works to be implemented under the project up to
about March 1991; in particular, the review would identify existing
criteria for establishing organization structure and staffing
requirements and evaluate whether the existing workloaa norms are
realistic.
Cc) Formulate guidelines and identify data collection required to
monitor, evaluate and compare various aspects of management perfor-
mance both in terms of physical and financial parameters (para 17).
(d) Undertake preliminary review of the following management problems
identified at appraisal: (i) changes in the organization into work
units which eliminate or phase out the separation of the AED staff
into Agriculture-Irrigation (A-I) and Agricultural-Mechanical (A-M)
cadres (para 7); (ii) separation of construction and U&M activities
(para 10); (iii) arrangements and procedures for using panchayats in
the management of minor irrigation units; (iv) problems related to
unionization or any other special interest group within the AED,
particularly where such grouping affects staff manning levels; and
(v) legislation, including terms and conditions of staff employment,
as a constraint to organization and staffing changes.
(e) Review of work activities to identify existing procedures (and effec-
tiveness of these procedures) in fixing responsibility and account-
ability of AED staff; evaluate the AED accounting procedures, iden-
tify shortcomings and recommend changes so that costs of AED opera-
tions can be broken down sufficiently to enable sound financial
management.



Annex 4
-102-                     Page 3
(f) Review present management training provisions and recozmend improve-
meats (para 19).
AED Mana2ement Determinants.
6.      In identifying and evaluating proposals to improve the management
capability of the AED, the following main determinants need special atten-
tion:
ResPonsibility of Ouerationai Circles
7.      Background. At present, there are 4 Agricultural-Irrigation (A-I)
circles responsible for tubewells and 4 Agricultural-Mechanical (A-M) circles
responsible for RLIs.  Within the territorial areas under their respon-
sibility, (A-I) and (A-M) circles undertake both construction and 0&1
activities. The State is divided into four parts such that one (A-I) and one
(A-M) circle serves the same territorial area. The consequences of the
territorial division of the State into separate organizations responsible for
specific types of works includes; (i) reduction in management efficiency
since staff have to cover large areas; and (ii) increase in O&M costs par-
ticularly with respect to transportation.
8.      Proiect Recoumendations. Divide the State into circles responsible
for both tubewells and RLIs and such that there is no territorial overlap-
ping. The area served by a work unit (circle, division or sub-division)
would be decided on the workload, namely, the density of existing tubevells
and XLIs and also take into account the construction program over the next
five years.
9.      Consultants- Task. The existing and proposed workload of the AED
would be analysed and the relative merits and practicality of any changes
assessed for the following: (i) continuing the present organization struc-
ture with separate work units for tubewells and BLI-s activities; and
(ii) re-organizing the field units on a territorial basis with mixed
workloads of tubewell and RLIs in each work unit depending on the local
spatial density of the various minor irrigation works.
Separation of Construction and O&M
10.     Background.  At present, organization and staffing requirements are
decided on norms of about Rs 0.4 M for an O&M division and about Rs 10.0 M
for a construction division. The O&M divisional norms include energy costs
which vary annually depending on irrigation demand. Where there is a con-
struction program, divisional staffing is adjusted to match the workload.
11.     Problem.  Since there is no separation between construction and O&M
responsibilities at any level in the organization, there is (and there will
continue to be) a variation in workload. With present accounting procedures,
it is virtually impossible to separate the costs of O&M and construction.
12.     Recommendations.  Construction and O&N activities should be made the
responsibility of separate work units at least up to sub-division level.
Within divisions with both construction and 0&M workloads, first the OE&
requirements should be fixed and work units staffed accordingly since this



-103-
Annex 4
Page 4
workload is firm and predictable. Staffing requirements for each
sub-division, division or circle would be estimated on the basis that (on
average) a division can manage about 400 units (either DTWs or RLIs). The
number of works managed by each division (or sub-divisi;-) would depend on
the spatial density of units to be managed and take into acci.nm provision
for future expansion if there is a construction program (on-goi:g or planned)
within the work unit area. The overriding consideration would be that works
can be managed effectively and efficiently. In those areas with a substan-
tial construction program, divisions would be strengthened by adding con-
struction sub-divisions or, if necessary, the number of divisions would be
increased. Staffing norms would depend on the spatial density and type of
the works. Extra staff, appropriately organized into work units would be
added to the basic O) structure with construction staffing based on an
average norm of Rs 10.0 H of works/division/year. The financial construction
norm per division should take into consideration the type of installations to
be constructed. The advantage of rationalizing the construction and O&M
activities and staffing would be that: Ci) it will be possible to separate
construction and OH accounts for management purposes; (ii) perfornumce can
be monitored and evaluated; and (iii) individual staff would knov -eir
responsibility, thus a degree of accountability can be introduced.
13.     Consultants  Task.  Review and evaluate the present situation and
establish whether there is sufficient information to check management perfor-
mance.  Formulate accounting procedures so that work unit performance in
terms of workload can be estimated and thus reviewed annually. Review the
recommendations given in para 12 above, and based on the conclusions reached
in such review, formulate a logical procedure for setting both cons:ruction
and O&M workload norms in physical and financial terms. Such norms would
take into account spatial density of works, allowances that should be made
for future workloads, and any other factors that may influence the perfor-
mance of a work unit.
The (A-1) and (A-H) Cadre Problem
14.     Backzround.  The AED staff are separated into two cadres (I and M)
and there is a complete demarcation of all AED activities into I and H jobs.
Although historically there may have been justification for the I and M
streaming, the protection of I and M jobs appears to restrict any change to
improve the management effectiveness and efficiency of the AED. The division
of AED staffing into two separate cadres is considered as an important reason
why the AED has become difficult to manage. Increase in the workloac of the
AED (and resulting O&M responsibilities) will make it increasingly more
difficult to resolve the problem as the AED expands.
15.     The Consultants would (inter alia) review the following actions which
may be taken by GOWB with respect to the (A-I) and (A-M) Cadre Problem with
an essential condition being to safeguard the career prospects of existing
staff:
(i)  All new recruits into the AED would be in a common cadre.
(ii) All existing positions which are designated I nr M, would remain I or
H slots and promotion to these slots would be based on seniority on
the respective I and M cadre lists.



-104-
Annex 4
Page 5
(iii)  All new posts created in the AED due to project both with respect to
construction and O&N would be designated 'common cadre- posts
irrespective of whether they are tubewell or RLI works. Since such
posts would have to be filled by a promotion, the post would be
filled on seniority basis by the most senior staff from either the
A-I or A-H cadre using a joint seniority list.
(iv)  All new posts in State level (Headquarters) created under the project
would be 'common cadre' since they have to deal with both tubewell
and RLI activities. The promotion to such a post would be as in item
(iii) above.
(v)  New specialist posts (for example, Officer in charge of a workshop)
would be restricted to qualified staff (i.e., Workshop
Officer-in-charge would be M cadre).
16.     Consultants- Task:  Formulate an appropriate, practical package
(including all or any of those outlined above) to address the A-I and A-H
cadre problem. The Consultants' proposal should identify the actions that
GOWB/AED would have to take to implement any changes proposed and a tentative
timetable for making such changes. A basic condition on any proposals would
be to safeguard the career prospects of existing AED staff.
Nonitorina and Evaluation
17.     The project would provide staff and facilities for 1&E of agricul-
tural, technical and socio-economic performance in the project area. The
existing M&E unit would be strengthened at headquarters of the AED under the
CE (Agriculture) with three field units set up at regional level. The H&E
unit would be responsible for collecting and analyzing data on project
implementation and performance particularly with respect to the quantity and
quality of the irrigation service.  The units would also be responsible for
agro-economic surveys, data collection for the main crops under the project
during the growth cycle including crop production, and input/crop yield data.
The operator at each tubewell or river lift system would assist che M&E units
by keeping certain daily and seasonal records which reflect aspects of
agricultural performance.
18.     Consultants' Task.  In the course of all studies undertaken by the
Consultants, they would identify activities which need to be monitored and
evaluated on a regularly basis to enable management performance to be
evaluated. In particular, they would decide: (i) the format and how much
data need be collected so that statistically analyzed any task to be
monitored can be; and (ii) recommend how, when and in what form, the monitor-
ing data should be made available to various levels of management. Thus,
once the task to be monitored has been identified, the Consultants would
assist the AED in the preparation of necessary data collection sheets, fre-
quency of collection of data, method of evaluation, how management can use
the proceeded data, and what feedback procedure should be instigated to pass
results back through the various levels of AED staff to the data collectors.
The specific management aim of monitoring and evaluation would to introduce
accountability for physical or financial performance of minor irrigation
development and any other activities undertaken by AED management.



-105-
Annex 4
Page 6
Train2ng
19.     At present, there are no formal management training courses in the
AED; it is assumed that as technical staff are promoted, they will learn
management techniques on the job. The consequence of this practise has been
little or no change of management procedures compared to those in force when
AED was set up. Since senior AED staff have no training in modern management
techniques, (particularly financial management), they have only been able to
make limited improvements to present management effectiveness.
20.     Consultants' Task.  The project provided funds for management train-
ing for senior AED staff. After the Management Study has been completed and
its recommendations generally agreed by lED/GOWB, the Consultants will
prepare short management courses for Senior AED staff and will also assist,
if required, in the running of such courses. The outline curricula for the
courses will be presented in the Final Report to be prepared by the Consult-
ants on completion of the Management Studies.
Conditions of Employment.
21.     The Consultant Group would be responsible to the Chief Engineer AED
and would be directed on a day-to-day basis through the Project Co-ordinator
or another suitable qualified and senior officer designated by the CE (AED).
A Management Study Committee, including Senior AED Officers and any other
representatives nominated by GOWB, would be set up and meet on a regular
basis to direct and to maintain close liaison with the Consultants as the
study proceeds. The Consultants are expected to be retained by September 30,
1985. Subject to the findings of the Preliminary Renort and the detailed
tasks that GtWJB/AED would like the Consultants to review and evaluate the
Consultants would prepare a draft Final Report by June 30, 1986. The draft
Final ReDort would be submitted to IDA for its review and comments and taking
into account IDA's comments, the GOWB/AED would undertake the recommendations
of the study to an agreed timetable. The timetable would be made available
to IDA for its review and comments by about September 30, 1986.
22.     The staffing of the the Consultant Group would consist of qualified
professionals in the field of Management Studies, sufficient to undertake the
tasks outlined in these draft Terms of Reference. In addition to general
management skills, the Consultants would require experts in the field of law,
administration, office equipment and facilities, and organization of Govern-
ment departments, particularly those following WB Public Work Department
procedures.
23.     The Consultants would provide sufficient transportation, equipment
and facilities to efficiently undertake the studies; the costs of these
requirements would be included in the fees agreed for the Consultancy. The
AED would provide the necessary assistance to enable the Consultant Group
to undertake its tasks and fulfil its obligations.
